Preface 
The following report is a 35 ECTS point Master Thesis. The present work has been carried out over a 
6-month period, from September 2014 to February 2015. The project was carried out under the 
supervision of Associate Professor Claus Sternberg and Associate Professor Mogens Kilstrup.

==PAGE:4==
Abstract 
Background: Staphylococcus aureus is a Gram-positive bacterium that is found in humans, other animals 
and in the surroundings. Together with Staphylococcus epidermidis, S. aureus is one of the most common 
bacteria that cause infections on implanted medical devices. S. aureus has an inherent ability to form 
biofilms on both biotic and abiotic surfaces, and the biofilm mode of growth contributes a high resistance 
towards antibiotics and clearance by the immune system. Biofilm growth on orthopedic implants causes for 
major complications, as they increase the risk of blood infections and because the growth might ruin the 
intentional purpose of the implant. These infections can be very difficult or even impossible to treat, and the 
device is usually removed and/or replaced to completely resolve the infection.   
It is estimated that more than 65 % of bacterial infections in humans are caused by biofilms, and it has now 
been acknowledged that biofilm formation is a very important aspect of most bacterial diseases. There is a 
rapid expansion worldwide in the development and usage of biomedical devices, which subsequently will 
increase the already major health and economic costs related to implant-associated biofilm infections.  This 
has increased the desire to obtain a greater understanding of the mechanisms and mediators that promote the 
biofilm mode of growth in S. aureus.   
Animal models are invaluable tools that allow investigation of bacterial infections in vivo, but the in vivo 
studies of staphylococcal biofilm formations are challenging, as the standard procedures used to visualize 
bacterial biofilms (staining with SYTO9) will stain eukaryotic cells as well as the biofilm cells. A solution 
to this problem is to fluorescently tag the strains of interest, and then subsequently to use them in in vivo 
studies. The only problem is, that genetic manipulation of S. aureus is an area of difficulty and that the 
current strategies used are very time consuming and show poor efficiency.  
Aim: The aim of the present study was to develop a step-by-step approach to obtain fluorescently tagged 
clinical S. aureus strains, which could be used to investigate staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro 
and in vivo. Enabling in vivo investigation of S. aureus biofilm formation will promote the understanding of 
the genetic regulations involved in biofilm formation and the pathology of the organism, which might aid in 
the process in finding new targets for therapeutics or in developing new anti-biofilm therapeutic agents.  
Results: Two different approaches were used to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical S. aureus strains:  
1. Fluorescent tagging with a fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid.  
2. Fluorescent tagging by integration of a fluorescent protein-expressing sequence into the chromosome. 
From the observations made during this study, it was realized that chromosomal integration of the 
fluorescent reporter gene is necessary when studying bacterial biofilm formation in vivo.  
The approach used to obtain chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-expressing strains, involved the 
usage of a fluorescent reporter gene-carrying single-copy vector, which would allow for chromosomal 
integration into a restriction deficient S. aureus strain (RN4220). A successful chromosomal integration of 
the fluorescent protein-expressing sequence was obtained, revealing fluorescently stable RN4220 strains. 
The integrated vector should then be transferred via bacteriophage-mediated generalized transduction from 
RN4220 and into the clinical S. aureus strains of interest, but due to time limitation this last step of the 
approach were not completed.  
Even though the last step in the approach was not completed, the successful chromosomal integration in the 
RN4220 intermediate cloning host, demonstrate that the fluorescent gene-carrying integration vectors can 
be exploited to chromosomally tag S. aureus strains. 
The approach used to integrate fluorescent genes into the chromosome of S. aureus strains can be used in 
general to genetically modify S. aureus strains, and might even present a usable approach in other 
Staphylococcus strains. 
	  
	  
==PAGE:5==
Resumé 
Baggrund: Staphylococcus aureus er en Gram-positiv bakterie der findes hos mennesker, dyr og i vores 
omgivelser. Sammen med Staphylococcus epidermidis, er S. aureus en af de mest almindelige bakterier der 
forårsager infektioner på medicinske implantater. S. aureus er en biofilmproducerende bakterie der kan 
vokse på både biotiske og abiotiske overflader, og bakterier der vokser i biofilm har en høj resistens overfor 
antibiotika og også mod at blive udryddet af immunsystemet.	  Biofilmvækst på ortopædiske implantater 
medfører komplikationer, da risikoen for infektioner i blodet øges og fordi biofilm vækst kan ødelægge det 
tilsigtet formål implantatet har. Biofilm-infektioner kan være meget vanskelige at behandle, og implantatet 
bliver normalt fjernet og/eller udskiftet for fuldstændigt at udrydde infektionen. 
Det anslås, at mere end 65 % af alle bakterielle infektioner hos mennesker er forårsaget af biofilm, og det er 
nu erkendt, at biofilmdannelse er et meget vigtigt aspekt i de fleste bakterielle sygdomme. Udvikling og 
brugen af biomedicinske implantater er støt stigende og de sundhedsmæssige og økonomiske omkostninger 
der er associeret med implantat-infektioner forventes derfor også at stige. Den forventede stigning har 
medført en stigende interesse i at få en større forståelse af de mekanismer og biofilm-formidlende agenter 
der fremmer biofilm vækst i S. aureus. 
Dyremodeller er et uvurderligt værktøj, der tillader forskning af bakterielle infektioner in vivo, men in vivo 
studier af stafylokok-biofilm vækst er en udfordrende opgave, da de standard procedurer er bliver anvendt 
til at visualisere bakterielle biofilm (farvning med SYTO9) farver eukaryote så vidt som prokaryote celler.	  
En løsning på dette problem er, at tagge de stammer man ønsker at studere med et fluorescerende gen, og så 
efterfølgende at bruge dem i in vivo studier. Det eneste problem er, at genetisk manipulation af S. aureus er 
meget vanskeligt og at de nuværende anvendte strategier er meget tidskrævende og viser dårlig effektivitet. 
Formål: Formålet med dette studie var, at udvikle en trinvis fremgangsmåde til at producere fluorescerende 
kliniske S. aureus-stammer, der kan anvendes til at undersøge stafylokok-biofilmdannelse både in vitro og 
in vivo. Det at muliggøre in vivo studier af S. aureus biofilmdannelsen, kan fremme forståelsen af den 
genetiske regulering der er involveret i biofilm dannelsen, samt medvirke til at finde nye terapeutiske 
angrebspunkter og at finde nye anti-biofilm terapeutiske midler. 
Resultater: To forskellige metoder blev anvendt til at producere fluorescerende kliniske S. aureus-stammer: 
1. Fluorescerende tagging vha. et fluorescerende protein-udtrykkende plasmid. 
2. Fluorescerende tagging hvor en fluorescerende protein-udtrykkende sekvens bliver integreret på 
kromosomet. 
De observationer der blev gjort i løbet af studiet, påviste at kromosomal-integration af det fluorescerende 
gen er nødvendigt hvis man skal studere bakteriel biofilmdannelse in vivo.  
Den metode der blev anvendt til at producere de kromosomalt-integrerede fluorescerende protein-
udtrykkende stammer gjorde brug af integrerings-vektorer der indeholdte et fluorescerede gen og som 
muliggjorde kromosomal integration i en S. aureus stamme der ikke kan udtrykke restriktionsenzymer 
(RN4220). Det viste sig at være muligt at bruge integrations-vektorer til at integrere et fluorescerende gen i 
kromosomet i RN4220 stammen, og stabile fluorescerede RN4220 stammer blev produceret. Målet var 
derefter at overføre den kromosomalt-integrerede fluorescens-udtrykkende vektor fra RN4220 kromosomet 
og ind i kliniske S. aureus stammer. Overførslen skulle ske vha. bakteriofag-medieret transduktion, men 
pga. tidsmangel blev det sidste trin i fremgangsmåde ikke gennemprøvet. Selvom det sidste trin ikke blev 
gennemprøvet, så viser den vellykkede kromosomale integration i RN4220 stammen, at fremgangsmåden 
kan udnyttes til at tagge S. aureus stammer kromosomalt. 
Den anvendte metode der blev brugt til at integrere et fluorescerende gen i kromosomet i S. aureus 
stammerne, kan anvendes i almindelighed til at genetisk modificere S. aureus-stammer, og måske endda 
også bruges til at modificere andre Staphylococcus-stammer.

1. Introduction 
Staphylococcus aureus is a versatile pathogen that is able to cause a wide array of human diseases ranging 
from superficial abscesses to more severe infections such as pneumonia, osteomyelitis, endocarditis and 
sepsis [Bose, 2014]. S. aureus infections strike people of all ages, but are most prevalent and cause more 
severe diseases in children, elderly people and in immunocompromised patients [Lowy, 1998]. The 
bacterium is notoriously known for its ability to develop resistance to antibiotics, and antimicrobial 
resistance has become one of the most serious public health concerns worldwide. It is especially the still-
rising emergence of methicillin-resistant S. aureus (MRSA) strains that has been the major area of focus in 
clinical medicine the past decade [Chambers & DeLeo, 2009]. In the past decade, the prevalence of 
community-acquired (CA)-MRSA infections in otherwise healthy individuals have increased. S. aureus is a 
prominent cause of bacterial infections and mortality worldwide, and the burden of diseases caused by S. 
aureus continues to grow [Chen et al., 2014; Lofti et al., 2014; Bose et al., 2013].  
S. aureus is an ubiquitous bacteria that has an inherent ability to form biofilms on both abiotic and biotic 
surfaces. Together with S. epidermidis, S. aureus is the leading etiological agent of infections on implanted 
medical devices [Lofti et al., 2014]. The biofilm mode of growth contributes a high resistance towards 
antibiotics and clearance by the immune system and conventional treatment strategies are unsuccessful in 
eradicating them. Implant related infections are a major complication in patients with orthopedic implants. 
Biofilm growth on implants causes a high risk of blood infections and the growth might ruin or disrupt the 
intentional purpose of the implant that then needs to be surgically removed or replaced [Montanaro et al., 
2011]. S. aureus is also the organism that provides the majority of osteomyelitis (bone infection) cases, 
which is a another complication for patients undergoing orthopedic surgeries. The specific role of 
staphylococcal biofilm in bone infections is unclear and there is an urgent need to clarify the pathology of 
osteomyelitis and also to find ways to either prevent or to treat these infections [Søe et al., 2013]. It is 
estimated that 65 % of bacterial infections in humans are caused by biofilms, and with an expected increase 
in the numbers of primary and revision arthroplasties worldwide, there is also an expected increase in the 
major health and economic costs associated with them [Lofti et al., 2014; Montanaro et al., 2011]. It is now 
acknowledged that biofilm formations are an important aspect of a vast majority of bacterial diseases, and 
the severe and pervasive impact of bacterial biofilms has increased the desire for a greater understanding of 
the complex interactions and regulatory processes behind the biofilm formation [Jefferson, 2004; Bose, 
2014].   
The ability to genetically manipulate bacterial pathogens is essential when investigating the mechanism of 
pathogenesis, but the current manipulation strategies used to genetically manipulate S. aureus are time 
consuming and show poor efficiency.  
The aim of the present study was to develop a step-by-step approach to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical S. 
aureus strains, which can be used to investigate staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro and in vivo. 
Enabling genetic manipulation and in vivo investigation of S. aureus biofilm formation will promote the 
understanding of the genetic regulation involved in biofilm formation, which might aid in the process in 
finding new targets for therapeutics or in developing new anti-biofilm therapeutic agents.   
2. Staphylococcus aureus  
Staphylococcus is a genus of Gram-positive bacteria that contain pathogens that are found in humans, other 
animals and in the surroundings. In humans, especially two species are prominent: S. aureus and S. 
epidermidis. S. aureus is an ubiquitous bacteria that is a part of the natural bacterial flora in approximately 
50% of the population, of which approximately 20% will carry the bacteria as a part of their normal floras 
==PAGE:9==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
4/66 
 
whereas the rest will carry the bacteria at times. The bacterium is an opportunistic pathogen that is frequently 
found on the skin and in the human respiratory tract. S. aureus is a facultative anaerobic coccal bacterium, 
which is also known as the "golden staph" because of the golden appearance of its colonies [Lowy, 1998; 
Archer et al., 2011]. S. aureus is an important human pathogen that can cause a wide variety of human 
infections, but he presence of S. aureus does not always indicate infection and it occurs as a commensal 
bacterium. S. aureus is the most common species of Staphylococcus to cause infections [Luong & Lee, 2007; 
Chien et al., 1999; Cheung et al., 2008; Chan & Foster, 1998; Fowler & Proctor 2014; Foster et al., 2014]. 
The typical infection route of S. aureus is through an epithelial breach, where the bacteria enter the body and 
spread via the bloodstream throughout the body. When the bacteria reach a suitable niche within the body it 
secrets a wide variety of cell wall-associated and extracellular proteins, which facilitates colonization. 
Whether the infection is contained or spreads, depends on a complex interplay between the S. aureus 
virulence determinants and the hosts defence mechanisms. S. aureus infections strike people of all ages, but 
are most prevalent and cause more severe diseases in children, elderly people and in immunocompromised 
patients [Lowy, 1998]. People that carry S. aureus as a part of their normal floras are at increased risk of 
nosocomial S. aureus infection compared with people that are not normally colonized [Lowy, 1998; Archer 
et al., 2011].  
Some of the properties that make S. aureus such successful pathogens is that it is able to invade a wide 
variety of cell types, that it can secrete a variety of toxins, and that it can form biofilms that contribute to a 
high resistance to antibiotics and clearance by the immune system, hence allows them to persist in the host. 
Its capability to cause a wide range of diseases in such diverse areas of the body, is most likely due to its 
potential to produce a large repertoire of virulence determinants, which is seen expressed during an infection 
processes [Gil et al., 2014]. S. aureus is an organism which is exceptional at adapting and surviving in many 
different environmental niches, and the organism regulates its virulence determinant biosynthesis as a 
response to the growth conditions. This regulation involves several global regulatory loci, of which the 
staphylococcal accessory regulator, sar, and the accessory regulator, arg, are the once that have been best 
characterized [Chan & Foster, 1998; Archer et al., 2011].  
In recent years, S. aureus has emerged as one of the most critical nosocomial pathogens, which is notorious 
for causing hospital-acquired infections and fatal infections that occur outside of health care settings [Gil et 
al., 2014, Kim et al., 2014]. Staphylococci are the most common cause of infections associated with medical 
implants, and it is predominately S. aureus and S. epidermidis that cause these infections. This statement was 
supported through a study made by Ariciola et al. (2005), where microbial strains were isolated from patients 
(n = 699) that had undergone revision surgery. The survey contained 1027 clinical isolates, of which 75.5% 
belonged to the Staphylococcus genus. Amongst these, S. aureus showed the highest prevalence with 35.5% 
of which 33.8% were related to medical device-associated infections [Lofti et al., 2014; Foster et al., 2014, 
Gil et al., 2014; Ariciola et al., 2005].

2.1. Treatment and Antibiotic Resistance 
The treatment of choice for a S. aureus 
infection is penicillin, but today the strain 
is notorious for its ability to become 
resistant towards most penicillin and 
other antibiotics. Antibiotic resistance in 
S. aureus was uncommon when penicillin 
was first introduced in 1943, but the 
bacterium has since showed its 
extraordinary way of developing 
resistance towards most used antibiotics 
(Figure 1) [Chambers & DeLeo, 2009]. 
Antibiotic treatment of infections caused 
by S. aureus is often unsuccessful owing 
to the development of antibiotic 
resistance, such as methicillin-resistant 
(MRSA) and vancomycin resistant S. 
aureus (VRSA). [Foster et al., 2014; 
Lowy, 1998]   
Staphylococcal resistance towards 
penicillin is mediated by the enzyme 
penicillinase, which cleaves the β-lactam ring of the penicillin molecules that leaves the antibiotic 
ineffective. β-lactam antibiotics that are penicillinase-resistant (such as methicillin, oxacillin, nafcillin) are 
all able to resist the degradation by the penicillinase, but unfortunately staphylococci (e.g. MRSA) have 
found a way of developing resistance towards these antibiotics as well [Chambers & DeLeo, 2009]. 
Resistance towards methicillin is mediated via the mec-operon that is spread on the staphylococcal cassette 
chromosome mec (SCCmec) genetic element, which is speculated to be transmissible across staphylococcal 
species via horizontal gene transfer [Lowy, 1998; Tsubakishita et al., 2010]. The origin of the mecA region is 
still unclear, but Tsubakishita et al. (2010) suggest that the SCCmec element was generated in 
Staphylococcus cells colonized in animals by acquiring the intrinsic mecA region from S. fleurettii, which is 
a commensal animal bacterium. Finding the origin of the mecA gene is important for the understanding of the 
evolution of the MRSA strains. The MRSA strains with the acquired mecA gene, encodes an altered 
penicillin-binding protein, PBP2a or PBP2’. This altered protein has a lower binding affinity for β-lactams 
that allows for resistance to all the β-lactam antibiotics including penicillin and cephalosporins, which 
obviates the clinical use during a MRSA infection. Aminoglycoside antibiotics (such as kanamycin, 
streptomycin, gentamycin etc.) were also once effective antibiotics, against staphylococcal infections, until 
the strains developed a mechanism to inhibit the aminoglycosides. MRSA is often treated with the 
glycopeptide vancomycin, but some strains have developed glycopeptide resistance by acquisitioning the 
vanA gene (originates from enterococci) that encodes for an enzyme that produce an alternative 
peptidoglycan that vancomycin cannot bind to [Tsubakishita et al., 2010]. By looking back at all the different 
antibiotics used to treat staphylococci infections, and all the different resistance developments that have 
occurred, it is very obvious that something must be done to eliminate the occurrence of new resistant strains 
and to fight the current resistant infections. It is necessary to impose restrictions of the use of antibiotics and 
other prescription drugs, and also to develop new drugs or new way to deliver therapeutics to fight the 
resistant infections [Moses et al., 2013; Foster et al., 2014].  
Figure 1 A graph showing the increasing burden of S. aureus resistance from 
1940 to today. The pink boxes indicate the introduction of a new antibiotic, 
while the blue boxes indicate the evolving resistant S. aureus strains 
[Chambers & DeLeo, 2009].

2.1.1. Prevalence of MRSA 
Antimicrobial resistance has 
become one of the most serious 
public health concerns 
worldwide, and it is especially 
the ever-increasing emergence 
of MRSA infection that has 
been a major area of focus the 
past decade [Chambers & 
DeLeo, 2009; WSSI, 2014]. 
Since the 1990s, the prevalence 
of MRSA in hospitals has 
exploded, and the prevalence of 
CA-MRSA has increased 
drastically the last decade. 
MRSA was first seen in 
England in 1961, and since 
then it has spread throughout 
the entire world [WSSI, 2014]. MRSA is of a major concern due to its potent virulence, which is coupled 
with it being resistant to many different antibiotics. When comparing infections caused by non-resistant 
staphylococci to infections caused by MRSA, there is a significant increase in the morbidity, the hospital 
admission time and convalescence, and also in the mortality in MRSA infections [Kim et al., 2014]. The 
evolution of MRSA has increased its prevalence parallel to that seen in penicillin-resistant S. aureus, and is 
now considered a pandemic. The prevalence of MRSA varies a lot by region or country, but it is clear that 
some Asian and North and South American countries are epicenters of antimicrobial resistance, with rates of 
> 50% (Figure 2). Infections caused by antimicrobial-resistant bacteria are often associated with 
inappropriate use of the antimicrobial therapeutics, and is often seen in countries or regions with very poor 
public health infrastructure. Antimicrobial 
resistance can easily spread between countries 
and continents due to long distance travels, 
and has become a global health concern [Kang 
& Song, 2013]. The incidence of MRSA 
infections in the Nordic countries (Denmark, 
Finland, Iceland, Norway and Sweden) is very 
low, but since around 2001-2003 a significant 
increase in the detected incidence have been 
observed (Figure 3) [Skov et al., 2005].  
With the increasing prevalence in MRSA 
infections, vancomycin or other glycopeptide 
antibiotics is the reserved treatment of choice. 
The first case of a vancomycin-resistant S. 
aureus was reported in 2002 in the United 
States, and has since then has been isolated in 
several different countries and continents (Iran, 
India, Latin America, Europe etc.), and VRSA 
Figure 2 The prevalence of methicillin resistant S. aureus (MRSA) isolates 
worldwide [Kang & Song, 2013].

has become a global phenomenon. The mechanism behind the development of the vancomycin resistance is 
still unknown, and just like with methicillin resistance, obtaining information about the gene(s) or regions 
that confer the resistance and their origin is important to be able to understand the evolution of the resistant 
strains. 
Even though the prevalence of VRSA is still relatively low, a VRSA outbreak resembling the penicillin-
resistant and MRSA outbreak is dreaded [Chang et al., 2003; Moses et al., 2013].  
2.2. Virulence Determinants 
As mentioned before S. aureus can cause a broad range of infections, and this variety is related to a large 
repetoire of virulence determinants that enable the organism to adhere to surfaces, to invade or avoid the 
immune system, and also to cause damaging toxic effects to the host. The virulence in S. aureus is 
multifactorial and caused by a combined action of several virulence determinants. The virulence 
determinants include enzymes, toxins (extracellular components) and antigens (cell wall components), of 
which some are mentioned in table 1 below [Mandal, 2012; Cue et al., 2012]. 
  
Table 1: A list of some of the S. aureus virulence 
determinants 
Enzymes 
Coagulase

Toxic Shock Syndrome Toxin 
Antigens 
Capsule 
 
Adhesins 
 
Almost all of the genes that encode virulence and resistance in staphylococci are found on the chromosome, 
as well as on extrachromosomal elements. These genes are transferred between staphylococcal strains, but 
also to and from other species through the extrachromosomal elements. The virulence determinant 
biosynthesis is regulates as a response to the growth conditions by at least six virulence and global 
regulators. Of these regulators, the regulatory loci sar and arg are the once that have been best characterized 
[Lowy, 1998; Chan & Foster, 1998]. Upon infection, S. aureus secrete many different surface-associated 
proteins, and during the initial stage of infection, the surface proteins that bind extracellular-matrix 
molecules and favours successful colonization of host tissues are predominantly expressed. The majority of 
the exoproteins, which favours the dispersal to adjacent tissue, are predominantly expressed during the post-
exponential/stationary phase. During infection, S. aureus will also produce enterotoxins that trigger major T 
cell responses that subsequently result in the production of pro-inflammatory cytokines that promote 
systemic inflammation and tissue destruction [Lowy, 1998].  
==PAGE:13==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
8/66 
 
As previously mentioned, S. aureus can form biofilms, which contribute to a high resistance towards 
antibiotics and clearance by the immune system. The impact of the biofilm formation on S. aureus virulence 
determinant expression is controversial and very little is known about the biofilm virulence determinants that 
enhance the establishment and long-term survival of biofilms in tissues [Phillips & Schultz, 2011].  
2.3. Biofilm  
Biofilms are surface-associated three-dimensional conglomerates of bacteria that are embedded within a self-
produced matrix of extracellular polymeric substances (EPS). The EPS consist of a complex mix of 
extracellular polysaccharides, proteins, lipids and DNA, and the EPS composition varies a lot between 
different biofilms. S. aureus is a notorious human pathogen, which cause some of the most common biofilm-
related infections in humans. S. aureus biofilms can form on host tissue and bone tissue, and the organism is 
also frequently associated with biofilm formation on medical devices such as prostheses, catheters, and 
portacaths [Kim et al., 2014]. Scientific interest in biofilm formation, eradication, and treatment has 
expanded the last two decades, and the knowledge of how physical aspects of the microenvironments of 
medical devices affect the biofilm dynamics is still poorly understood. The more we learn about the genetic 
regulations of biofilm formation, the more we understand about the “forces” that drive the switch to biofilm 
mode of growth [Jefferson, 2004].  
Why is it that bacteria form biofilmsThe only true driving force that can make an organism change, is if the 
change is beneficial to its reproductive fitness, and any action that result in an increased survival and cell 
proliferation will endure within a species. The formation of biofilm must therefore promote survival and 
propagation of the cell. Genetic adaption is the cornerstone of survival and fitness, and adaptions occur when 
new genetic material is obtained, if mutations or recombination happens within already existing genes, or 
from regulation of already existing genetic material. The ability of a bacterium to form and grow as a biofilm 
is one example of bacterial adaption, where the cell though gene expression-alterations of already existing 
genes, promote the biofilm mode of growth. Especially bacteria that are able to colonize humans, such as S. 
aureus, have shown to be very environmentally adaptable, since they must be able to shift between living in 
the environment and in the human host [Lofti et al., 2014; Foster et al., 2014; Jefferson, 2004].  
It is estimated that more than  65% of bacterial infections seen in humans are caused by biofilms, and once a 
biofilm have developed, the removal is challenging. Biofilms are often responsible for chronic infections, 
which lead to high morbidity and major healthcare costs [Kim et al., 2014; Lofti et al., 2014; Foster et al. 
2014]. Biofilms can tolerate antimicrobial agents at concentration that are 10-1,000 times higher than the 
once needed to kill genetically equivalent planktonic bacteria, which is why the removal of a developed 
biofilm is so challenging [Jefferson, 2004]. 
The thickness of a biofilm can range from a single cell layer to a substantial community, and biofilms can be 
multi-species or even multi-kingdom. The composition of biofilms varies a lot depending on the site of the 
infection and on the strain(s) that compose the biofilm [Jefferson, 2004; Arhcer et al., 2011; Cue et al., 
2012]. The formation of a biofilm is a dynamic process that occurs through sequential steps. As seen in 
figure 4, the first step in a biofilm formation is the initial attachment of a planktonic bacterium to a surface. 
The bacteria will then proliferate and accumulate, and form multi-layered cell clusters. The bacterial clusters 
become enclosed in a self-constructed extracellular polymeric matrix consisting of EPS, which provides for 
protection of the bacteria within the biofilm. As the biofilm matures and ages, bacterial cells can escape the 
biofilm, and spread throughout the host as a planktonic cell. This planktonic cell can then find a new location 
in the host where it can form a new biofilm [Gil et al., 2014; Cue et al., 2012]. Structural analyses have  
==PAGE:14==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
9/66 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shown that the structure of a biofilm, can vary a lot depending on the environmental conditions [Archer et 
al., 2011].  
The initial attachment of staphylococci to a surface within a human or an animal is usually facilitated by cell 
surface proteins that are able to bind plasma proteins such as fibrinogen, fibronectin, collagen etc. These cell 
surface proteins can be referred to as microbial surface components recognizing adhesive matrix molecules 
(MSCRAMMs) [Cue et al., 2012; Jefferson, 2004; Lowy, 1998]. Studies have showed that many 
staphylococci are capable to bind directly to plastic surfaces, and S. aureus has an inhered ability to form 
biofilms on both biotic and abiotic surfaces [Lofti et al., 2014].  
When comparing the gene expressions in biofilm-associated bacteria to that of a free-living cell, there is a 
significant change in the expression of a wide variety of extracellular compounds, which have been 
identified as mediators of the staphylococcal biofilms. Amongst these mediators are the poly-N-
acetylglucosamine exopolysaccharide (PNAG), extracellular DNA (eDNA), and surface-associated proteins 
[Gil et al., 2014]. Due to the major health and economic costs associated to biofilm infections, there is an 
increasing attention in understanding bacterial biofilms, its mediators and its growth in humans [Archer et 
al., 2011]. Especially the regulatory mechanisms behind the formation and dissolution have been extensively 
researched, aiming at developing and finding targets for anti-biofilm therapeutic agents [Jefferson, 2004; 
Archer et al., 2011].  
2.3.1 Implant Related Infections 
As mentioned, S. aureus is one of the most common bacteria that cause infections associated with medical 
implants. These types of infections cause a major economical and medical cost and burden to the health care 
system. Several different control measures and treatments have been developed against the implant-
associated infections, but these have simply not been able to completely eliminate the infections.  
As previously mentioned, the knowledge of how physical aspects of medical devices affect the biofilm 
dynamics is still poorly understood. What is known, is that when a foreign device, such as a medical implant 
is introduced into a body, the minimum inoculum size needed to cause an infection is reduced by a factor of 
more than 100,000, which means that a microorganism can cause an infection with a inoculum size as small 
as 100 colony forming units (CFU) [Lowy, 1998; Montanaro et al., 2011]. 
Implanted medical devices get coated with plasma proteins almost immediately after insertion into the body, 
Figur 4 The formation of a biofilm occurs in several stages. 1: Initial attachment of a planktonic bacterium to a 
surface. 2+3: Proliferation and accumulation in multi-layered cell clusters. 4: The bacteria become enclosed in a 
self-constructed extracellular polymeric matrix. 5: A cell escapes the mature biofilm and moves as a planktonic 
cell to another location, where it can attach and form a new biofilm [Emeryville, 2013].  
==PAGE:15==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
10/66 
 
which provides a host derived serum constituent layer of which enables staphylococci to adhere through the 
MSCRAMMs [Lofti et al., 2014; Archer et al., 2011]. The infection can happen during implantation, during 
subsequent trauma or hematogenously.  
Biofilm growth on implanted medical devices are not only causing a high risk of blood infection, but the 
biofilm growth might also ruin or disrupt the intentional purpose of the implant. Due to the difficulties 
treating S. aureus implant related infections the device is usually removed to completely resolve the infection 
[Archer et al., 2011]. 
Millions of indwelling medical devices are implanted into patients every year and S. aureus is the main cause 
for their infection and failure.  
S. aureus provides the majority of osteomyelitis cases seen and it is a significant complication for patients 
undergoing orthopaedic surgeries, especially fracture fixation and arthroplasty. This means that a S. aureus 
biofilm formation on implants, in addition to possibly destroying or reducing the functionality of the implant 
also in many cases cause related problems such as osteomyelitis, which worsens the patients health [Archer 
et al., 2011; Reizner et al. 2014]. 
The specific role of staphylococcal biofilm in bone infections is unclear and there is an urgent need to clarify 
the pathology of S. aureus-associated osteomyelitis and also to find ways to either prevent or to treat these 
infections [Søe et al., 2013; Gil et al., 2014]. There is an expected increase in the numbers of primary and 
revision arthroplasties worldwide, which automatically leads to an expected increase in the major health and 
economic costs associated with them [Lofti et al., 2014; Montanaro et al., 2011]. 
Osteomyelitis is a big problem in S. aureus implant related infections, because the arg quorum-sensing 
system that regulates the dispersal of biofilm cells and the expression of arg-controlled virulence factors 
upregulate the production of immunoavoidance factors, toxins and other arg-controlled virulence factors, 
which result in tissue and bone damage (osteomyelitis). As mentioned, during infections S. aureus produce 
enterotoxins that promote systemic inflammation and tissue destruction [Lowy, 1998]. 
Obtaining a better understanding of the biofilm formation and pathogenesis, hence the virulence factors of S. 
aureus, will aid in the development of new antimicrobial agents. Given the many difficulties in studying S. 
aureus infections in humans, animal models serve as a great alternative in exploring the pathogenesis in vivo. 
Animal models are used to mimic the clinical scenarios seen in patients as much as possible. Depending on 
the species investigated, and also on the setting of the infection, different animal models are used. Even 
though no ideal animal model exists, in vivo studies performed on S. aureus have showed more reliable and 
precise results than in vitro experiments. 
Many different animals have been used as a model to study infections caused by Staphylococci, and Søe et 
al. (2013) have developed a rat model that is used to investigate the pathology of bone and bone marrow 
infections caused by S. aureus following insertion of a knee prosthetics [Søe et al., 2013]. 
2.3.1.1. Rat Prosthesis Model of Implant Related Osteomyelitis 
The rat model was developed by Søe et al. to investigate implant-related osteomyelitis that mimics that seen 
in human osteomyelitis. There is an urgent need investigate and to explore approaches to prevent or to treat 
osteomyelitis, and this animal model is a great tool for that [Søe et al., 2013]. 
The model can also be used to investigate where S. aureus reside and spread on implants or bones during an 
infection. A more detailed description of the rat prosthesis model, which was used in the present study, is 
found in the section “In Vivo Rat Prosthesis Model”.

2.4. Biofilm Regulation by Matrix Polymers: PNAG and eDNA 
A detailed description of how sarA, arg and sigB 
regulates the development of a biofilm is found in 
appendix A.  
The more knowledge we have about the genetic 
regulations of biofilm formation, the more we 
understand about the force that drives the switch 
from planktonic growth to biofilm mode of growth. 
The regulation and production of PNAG and 
extracellular DNA (eDNA) has shown to have 
major regulatory effects on some strains. 
Until recently, it was thought that the accumulation 
of bacteria during the formation of a biofilm was 
attributed only to the polysaccharide intercellular 
adhesion, PNAG, which is encoded by the ica-
operon (Figure 6) [Foster et al., 2014]. PNAG has 
been found to be essential for biofilm formation by many strains of S. aureus and S. epidermidis, but a 
number of staphylococcal strains exhibit PNAG-independent biofilm formation. It has now been shown that 
staphylococcal surface proteins can promote the accumulation of bacteria even in an ica-independent 
manner. In these strains, secreted proteins and eDNA appear to substitute for the precense of PNAG [Cue et 
al., 2012; Wu & Xi, 2009]. In some of these PNAG-deficient S. aureus strains a strong biofilm phenotypes is 
seen, and recently eDNA has shown to comprise an important structural component in the complex 
regulation of S. aureus biofilm formation [Archer et al., 2011; Izano et al., 2008].  
2.4.1. Extracellular DNA 
In a study performed by Izano et al., (2008), eDNA showed to be a major structural component of the 
biofilm matrix in some S. aureus strains and a minor one in some S. epidermidis biofilms. The eDNA did 
also show to have a function in cell-to-cell interconnection during the development of the biofilm. Enzymatic 
degradation of extracellular DNA has shown to weaken the biofilm structure, resulting in the release of 
biofilm cells from the surface of the biofilm [Izano et al., 2008].  
As it is seen in figure 5, the presence of eDNA enhance the biofilm formation, while the presence of DNase 
inhibits the eDNA-mediated biofilm formation. The expression of the two genes, lrg and cidA controls 
whether or not a cell should undergo lysis, hence release eDNA. Both lrg and cidA works as regulators of the 
murein hydrolase and thereby the cellular lysis. An upregulation of the expression of lrg result in an 
inhibition of cellular lysis, while the expression of cidA enhance cellular lysis. When a cell lyse, it releases 
eDNA, which then enhance the biofilm formation [Archer et al., 2011].  
It is a common phenomenon that eDNA is released mainly from cell lysis, but several studies have 
discovered that there might be other ways that eDNA is secreted. These indicate that eDNA might be 
secreted actively via transport vesicles with the purpose of creating the biofilm matrix. Further research is 
still needed to clarify the origin and exact role of eDNA in the biofilm structure. The further characterization 
of the composition of eDNA and its role in the biofilm development, can eventually lead to a better 
understanding and possibly lead to a development of new drugs or targets to fight biofilm infections [Wu & 
Xi, 2009]. 
Figure 5 The result of eDNA in biofilm formation. The 
presence of eDNA is enhance the biofilm formation, while 
the presence of DNase inhibits eNDA-mediated biofilm 
formations. The presence of eDNA is regulated by two 
genes lrg and cidA, that through the muerin hydrolase 
inhibits or enhance cellular lysis, respectively. The presence 
of eDNA might also be secreted actively via transport 
vesicles [Archer et al., 2011]. 
==PAGE:17==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
12/66 
 
Due to the fact that it was recently thought that PNAG was essential in the formation of biofilms, a lot of 
studies have been performed on the ica-operon in S. aureus strains [Archer et al., 2011; Foster et al., 2014; 
Jefferson, 2004].  
2.4.2. The ica-Operon 
 
 
 
  
As mentioned, PNAG, is encoded by the ica-operon (Figure 6). The intercellular adhesion locus, icaADBC, 
encodes gene products that are responsible for the synthesis of PNAG, which is a β-1,6 linked poly-N-
acetylglucosamine polymer [Jefferson, 2004]. PNAG is synthesized by the products of four genes, icaADBC, 
which is encoded in a single operon. IcaADBC is encoded by a single transcript, whereas the fifth gene, 
icaR, is transcribed divergently from the other ica genes. icaR encodes a transcriptional negative regulator, 
that represses the transcription of the icaADBC. In vitro investigation of the production of PNAG, show that 
it is primarily regulated at the transcriptional level. The signals controlling PNAG production in vivo are not 
clearly defined yet, but it is seen that a number of environmental conditions affect the production in vitro. 
Amongst these are, high temperature, anaerobiosis, high osmolarity, glucose, and ethanol, that all result in an 
induction of the PNAG production. Subinhibitory concentrations of specific antibiotics, which include 
gentamicin, tetracycline, and the streptogramins, quinopristin and dalfopristin, can also increase the 
production of PNAG. There is a strain-to-strain variation in regard to which environmental conditions result 
in an increase of the PNAG production [Cue et al., 2012; Archer et al., 2011].  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6 The ica-operon. icaADBC encodes gene products that is responsible for the synthesis of PNAG. IcaADBC is encoded by a 
single transcript. IcaR is transcribed divergently from the other ica genes. icaR encodes a repressor that represses the transcription 
of icaADBC.   
Figure 7 Regulatory factors involved in the S. aureus biofilm formation. The blue square 
contains the regulatory factors involved in PNAG-dependent biofilm formation. The orange 
square contains the regulatory factors involved in PNAG-independent biofilm formation. 
The expression of icaADBC result in expression of PNAG, hence the formation of biofilm. 
The expression of icaR and tcaR supress the expression of icaADBC.  
Spx induce the expression of icaR, while Rbf inhibits the expression of icaR. 
SrrAB is induced under anaerobic conditions and causes expression of icaADBC. 
SpA, FnBPs and Bap can mediate biofilm formation [Archer et al., 2011].  
==PAGE:18==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
13/66 
 
There is a long list of factors that affect the expression of the ica-operon, which make the regulation very 
complex and multifunctional. Many S. aureus strains do not produce PNAG in vitro even though they 
encode icaADBC. The various factor included in the regulatory mechanisms differ considerably between 
different strains as well as between species. A complete description of how the PNAG synthesis is induced 
during an infection still remains relatively unknown, but a few regulatory factors involved in the S. aureus 
biofilm formation have been identified (Figure 7) [Cue et al., 2012].  
          2.4.2.1. PNAG-dependent Biofilm Formation 
As it is seen in the blue box in figure 7, the expression of the icaADBC gene cluster result in 
the expression of PNAG and thereby biofilm formation in PNAG-dependent biofilms. This 
expression is suppressed by the production of icaR and tcaR (transcriptional regulator of the 
teicoplanin-associated locus), which work by negatively regulating the expression of 
icaADBC. The two proteins Spx (global regulator of stress response genes) and Rbf (protein 
regulator of biofilm formation), induces and inhibits icaR expression, respectively, thereby up- 
and downregulating the expression of PNAG.  
The staphylococcal respiratory response regulator, SrrAB, is another regulatory factor, and is 
induced under anaerobic conditions. The presence of SrrAB causes expression of the icaADBC 
gene cluster, PNAG production and biofilm formation [Archer et al., 2011].  
          2.4.2.2. PNAG-independent Biofilm Formation 
As it is seen in the orange square in figure 7, S. aureus strains that contains an ica-deletion, are 
not able to synthesize PNAG. The biofilm formation are then mediated through cell wall-
associated protein cell-to-cell adhesion (Only observed in MRSA). Amongst these proteins 
are: S. aureus protein A (SpA), fibronectin-binding proteins (FnBPs) and biofilm associated 
protein (Bap) [Archer et al., 2011].   
The regulation, formation, maintenance, and dispersal of biofilms have showed to be very complex and 
multifunctional, and a lot of areas are still unstudied. The eradication and prevention of biofilm formations 
still yield major health and economical costs, and further investigation within the subject is needed to 
hopefully one day to being able to developing a new anti-biofilm therapeutic agent or to find a specific 
biofilm target to prevent or eradicate the biofilm formations.  
3. Genetic Manipulation of Staphylococci 
The ability to genetically manipulate bacteria is essential when investigating the function of a gene/gene 
product or when trying to understand the physiology of an organism. Genetically manipulation of S. aureus 
for molecular experimentation is an area of difficulty, and many of the current manipulation strategies are 
difficult to perform and show poor efficiency. The strong restriction barriers present in S. aureus limits the 
possibility of genetic manipulation of the bacteria. Bacteriophage-mediated transduction has greatly 
increased the possibilities of successful genetic manipulations of both S. aureus and S. epidermidis, and has 
facilitated investigations of clinically relevant isolates [Bose, 2014; Olson & Horswill, 2014]. 
Investigation of S. aureus biofilm formation in vivo is challenging because the standard procedure used to 
visualize bacterial biofilms (staining by SYTO9), will stain eukaryotic cells as well as the biofilm cells. 
Obtaining an efficient method to introduce fluorescent genes into clinical S. aureus strains will provide a 
very useful microbiological tool to investigate S. aureus biofilm formations in vitro and in vivo.  
 
==PAGE:19==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
14/66 
 
The aim of this project was to develop a step-by-step approach to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical 
S. aureus strains, which can be used to investigate staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro and 
in vivo. The obtained approach can also be used in general to modify S. aureus strains, and might even 
present a usable approach in other staphylococcus strains, such as S. epidermidis.    
To obtain the fluorescently tagged clinical S. aureus isolates, two separate approaches were used and 
evaluated: 
1. Transformation of a fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid into clinical S. aureus strains  
2. Chromosomally integrating a fluorescent-expressing plasmid into clinical S. aureus strains  
During the completion of the experiments the usage of bacteriophage-mediated generalized transduction and 
a single-copy integration vector system was used. The principles behind these techniques are described 
below.  
3.1. Bacteriophage-Mediated Transduction 
Transduction is the process where DNA is 
transferred from one bacterium to another via a 
bacteriophage. This process was discovered by 
Norton Zinder and Joshua Lederberg in 1951, 
and the generalized transduction is now a 
convenient tool that is used to move mutations, 
plasmids or chromosomally encoded markers 
between strains. The method behind the 
principle is based on the phenomenon that when 
a bacteriophage infects and replicates within a 
bacterium, it will occasionally assemble and 
mistakenly package bacterial DNA instead of 
the viral genome (Figure 8). The lytic cycle 
leads to a bloom of progeny bacteriophage 
particles, which are released to the surroundings 
by lysis of the host cell. The phage that carries the bacterial DNA can then infect another bacteria and inject 
the packaged bacterial DNA into the new bacterium. The DNA from the first bacteria can integrate into the 
chromosome of the recipient bacteria by homologous recombination or it can recircularize into a plasmid 
[Olson & Horswill, 2014].   
Research methods used for bacterial strain construction have taken advantage of the mistakenly packaging 
and carrying of bacterial DNA, to genetically manipulate recipient strain. Bacteriophage mediated 
transduction is commonly used to manipulate S. aureus genomes [Olson & Horswill, 2014].  
During the bacteriophage-mediated transduction that was performed in this project the Φ11- and Φ80a-phage 
was used. The staphylococcal bacteriophage Φ11 is originally isolated from S. aureus 8325, and Φ80a phage 
is a hybrid of Φ11 and Φ80 [McNamara, 2008]. They are both temperate phages that are capable of both a 
lytic and lysogenic lifecycle. Both phages used in this project package bacterial DNA instead of the viral 
genome quite frequently compared to other phages, which is favourable when using them for bacteriophage-
mediated transduction [Ye  & Lee, 1992]. 
Figure 8 A simplified illustration of bacteriophage-mediated transdcution. 
The picture is taken from the bacteriophage-mediated transduction 
protocol found in appendix B. 
==PAGE:20==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
15/66 
 
3.2. Single-Copy Integration Vectors 
The single-copy integration vectors (pCL25, pLL29 and pLL39 (Figure 15)) that are used in this study are 
based on the lysogenic bacteriophage L54a and Φ11 site-specific recombination system. The vectors are able 
to replicate autonomously in E. coli, but the vectors does not possess the staphylococcal replication function. 
For the vectors to be able to survive in staphylococci, it needs to integrate into the chromosome. The 
integration vector pCL25 contains an L54a_attB site, while the pLL29 and pLL39 contains both an L54a and 
Φ11_attB site. The pLL39 integration vector is different from pLL29, because it contains a five tandem 
repeats of the T1 transcriptional terminator that is supposed to prevent the expression of the reporter gene 
from an external promoter. A plasmid map of the three integration vectors are found in figure 15 [Luong & 
Lee, 2007]. 
The integration vectors integrate into the staphylococcal chromosome via site-specific recombinase. As 
mentioned, the integration vectors contains either one or both the Φ11- and an L54a-attP site, which allows 
for site-specific recombination into either the Φ11- or L54a-attB site on the bacterial chromosome. As it is 
seen on figure 9, a phage-encoded integrase (Int), is 
needed during integrative recombination [Ye & Lee, 
1992]. 
The Φ11- and L54a_attB sites, where the different 
integration vector will integrate into the bacterial 
chromosome, have been mapped on a genetic map of 
S. aureus NTCC8325. The L54a_attB site is found to 
be within the geh gene, which encodes a lipase. 
Disrupting this gene may influence the virulence of 
the bacteria, which is why vectors carrying the Φ11 
attP site were constructed. The Φ11_attB site is found 
near the L54a attP site in an intergenic region 
between the purB and metA loci, which has shown not 
to influence the bacterial phenotype [Ye & Lee, 
1992]. 
In this study the single-copy integration vectors were 
used to carry a fluorescent reporter gene into S. 
aureus strains. The constructed fluorescent protein-
expressing integration vectors were manipulated and 
amplified in E. coli, and due to the highly efficient 
staphylococcus restriction barriers the integration vectors cannot be transferred directly into S. aureus strains. 
The majority of S. aureus isolates exhibit intrinsic resistance to transformation with plasmids that have been 
purified from E. coli. This is due to a very strong restriction modification barrier, of which the main barriers 
to transformation in S. aureus are: 
- Type IV restriction: The cytosine methylations on the E. coli isolated plasmids is recognized and restricted 
by the S. aureus restriction enzyme SauUSI. 
- Type I (of restriction modifications): S. aureus encodes a host specificity determinant (hsd), adenine 
methylation. DNA without this host specificity adenine methylation is recognized as foreign and is restricted 
[Baba et al., 2008].   
Figure 9 A simplified figure showing the phage 
integration and excision mechanism. The site-specific 
recombination occurs between the attP site (purple) on 
the phage DNA and the attB site (Brown) in the 
bacterial chromosomal.  
==PAGE:21==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
16/66 
 
Due to the very efficient restriction barriers, it is not possible to transform the integration vectors that have 
been amplified in E. coli directly into the clinical S. aureus isolates. To overcome this problem a restriction-
deficient S. aureus strain (S. aureus RN4220) is used as the intermediate cloning host, before the integration 
vector is transferred into the clinical S. aureus isolates.  
3.3. Staphylococcus aureus RN4220 
S. aureus RN4220 is a non-lysogenic restriction-deficient strain, which is an efficient recipient for E. coli 
DNA [Ye & Lee, 1993, Charpentier et al., 2004]. S. aureus RN4220 is a commonly used laboratory strain 
that is characterized by its mutation in the sau1 hsdR gene, which makes it restriction deficient and hence an 
ideal intermediate cloning host. S. aureus RN4220 was originally derived from the S. aureus strain 
NCTC8325-4 by using nitrosoguanidine (MNNG) mutagenesis (chemical and UV mutagenesis) [Nair et al., 
2011].  
The clinical isolate NCT8325 strain originally contains three prophages, Φ11, Φ12, and Φ13, which can be 
induced from the bacteria with mitomycin C (Protocol is found in appendix C). NCT8325 was stepwise 
UV-cured of all three resident prophages and strain NCT8325-4 was obtained. Through verbal transmission 
form Jakob Krause Haaber, I was informed that the prophages were removed via prophage excision, hence 
removed completely from the bacterial genome, leaving the attP sites in the chromosome [Bæk et al., 
2013].   
Studies performed by Nair et al. (2011) shows that RN4220 harbours additional mutations to the sau1 hsdR 
gene mutation, which affect virulence genes as well as genes involved in fitness and numerous stress-
associated putative regulators, which shows that virulence studies performed with RN4220 should proceed 
with caution.

Clinical S. aureus Isolates 
 
Among the clinical S. aureus isolates, that were fluorescently tagged and studied in this 
project, are the following S. aureus MN8 and Newman strains:  
 
 
 
 
 
S. aureus MN8 is a CP8 clinical isolate from a Toxic Shock Syndrome patient that was 
isolated by Dr. Patrick Schlievert (University of Minnesota, MN). S. aureus MN8 is great 
at forming osteomyelitis. 
S. aureus Newman is a CP5 clinical strain isolated from an infection in a human patient, 
and it shows robust virulence properties in animal models of disease.  
The ica locus was replaced with a tetracycline resistance cassette via homologous 
recombination to obtain the ica- strains (ica::tet) [Mackey-Lawrence et al., 2009; Kropec et 
al., 2005].  
4.2. Bacterial growth conditions: The E. coli strains were grown at 37°C in Luria Bertani (LB) 
broth at 200 rpm or on LB plates - unless otherwise stated. For plasmid maintenance or plasmid 
selection, the media were supplemented with the following antibiotics: 100 μg/mL ampicillin, or 25 or 
50 μg/mL spectinomycin.  
The S. aureus strains were grown at 37°C in tryptic soy broth (TSB) at ~200 rpm or on tryptic soy agar 
(TSA) plates – unless otherwise stated. In the biofilm flow chamber assays, S. aureus biofilm media was

4.3. Fluorescent Tagging of Clinical S. aureus Strains 
Bacteriophage-mediated generalized transduction was used to transfer a fluorescent protein-expressing 
plasmid from one S. aureus strain (donor strain) and into selected clinical S. aureus strains (recipient 
strains. Figure 10 shows a simplified illustration of the bacteriophage-mediated generalized transduction 
that was used to transfers the plasmid from one bacterium to another.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 10 Bacteriophage-mediated generalized transduction. A simplified figure showing 
the 7 overall steps used when transferring a plasmid from one S. aureus strain (Donor 
strain) and into another S. aureus strain (Recipient strain) via bacteriophages.  
1 The bacteriophage attaches and injects its phage genome into the bacteria (Donor 
bacteria) 
2 Phage-specific enzymes break down the bacterial chromosome 
3 Phage enzymes and bacterial materials are used to replicate the phage chromosome 
4 Phage genes are expressed and used to produce phage structural components 
5 Progeny phages assemble and DNA is packaged in the phage heads. Subsequently the 
bacterium is lysed.  
6 The phage that carries the mistakenly packaged bacterial plasmid can infect and inject 
the DNA into another bacterium (Recipient strain).  
7 The bacterial DNA circularizes and forms a plasmid that floats in the bacterial 
cytoplasm 
 
==PAGE:26==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
21/66 
 
In an experiment like this, it is favourable to use a bacteriophage that is not good at distinguishing 
between its own genome and bacterial DNA, and amongst the less successful phage-chromosome 
recognizing bacteriophages is the Φ11-phage.  
4.3.1. Donor Strain 
The donor strains (Table 3) used in this experiment is S. aureus strains that contain cytoplasmic fluorescent 
protein-expressing plasmids. The two different donor strains are constitutively expressing either YFP or 
GFP. The donor strains were provided by Postdoc Jakob Krause Haaber, and the experiment were conducted 
in cooperation with Jakob Krause Haaber at the Department of Veterinary Disease Biology at the University 
of Copenhagen.

S. aureus JH857

gfp-gene 
4.3.2. Recipient Strain 
The recipient strains used in this experiment were the selected clinical S. aureus strains (Table 4).

4.3.2.1. Sensitivity Towards Chloramphenicol and Erythromycin 
Prior to the bacteriophage-mediated transduction, the recipient strains were tested for their sensitivity 
towards chloramphenicol and erythromycin. It is important that the recipient strains are sensitive towards 
these antibiotics, because once the transduction is completed, antibiotic selection is used to screen for the 
plasmid-receiving recipient strains. 
4.3.3. Bacteriophage-Mediated Generalized Transduction 
A detailed protocol that describes the individual steps and materials that were used during the preparation of 
the phage lysate and when the transduction was performed is found in appendix B.  
4.3.3.1. Preparing the Phage Lysate 
The first step in transferring the gfp- and yfp-carrying plasmids (pCM29 and pJEBAN3) into the clinical 
isolates was to prepare Φ11-phage lysates. The Φ11 phages and the donor strains were mixed together and 
CaCl2 was added, as calcium is needed for the progeny phages to assemble. After incubation for about 5 
min., the phages had infected the donor bacteria and the phage- and bacteria-containing mixture was added 
to 3 mL molten TSB-top agar supplemented with additional CaCl2. It is very important that the top agar has 
cooled down to less than 45oC before use, to ensure a successful preparation of the phage lysate. The top 
==PAGE:27==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
22/66 
 
agar mixture is then poured onto TSA and incubated at 37oC O/N. The next day plaques were visible in the 
top agar, which illustrates that the Φ11phages have infected the donor bacteria, replicated its viral genome, 
produced progeny phages and then lysed the bacteria (Lytic life cycle). During the packaging of the phage 
genome into the progeny phages, a small fraction will mistakenly have packaged bacterial DNA instead, and 
of those bacterial DNA-carrying phages, an even smaller fraction will carry the fluorescent protein-
expressing plasmid of interest (Figure 10).  
The last step in the preparation of the phage lysate was to purify the phages from the bacteria-containing top 
agar. This was done through centrifugation and sterile filtration according to the protocol found in appendix 
B.  
During transduction, S. aureus is very sensitive to the ratio between phages and bacteria and the multiplicity 
of infection (MOI) should be around 0.1. To ensure the right MOI, the concentrations of plaque forming 
units (PFU) in the transducing phage lysates were determined before the actual transduction was performed. 
A protocol that describes the method used to determine the PFU is found in appendix B.  
4.3.3.2. Transduction  
The prepared transducing phage lysates were then used to infect the recipient strains. When the fluorescent 
protein-expressing plasmid-carrying phages infected the recipient strains, they would inject the plasmid into 
the cell (Step 6 in figure 10).  
The tittered transducing phage lysate was mixed with the recipient strains to achieve a MOI of around 0.1, 
which is 10 bacteria to 1 bacteriophage. This specific MOI will minimize the amount of double infections 
and increase the chances of a successful transduction. The bacteria and phage mixture was incubated at 37o C 
and 200 rpm for no longer than 20 min. This incubation time allows the bacteriophages to adhere to the 
recipient-bacteria and to inject its DNA into it, - but not for the bacteriophage to run a full lytic replication 
cycle and lyse the cell. After the incubation, ice-cold sodium citrate was added to the mixture to stop the lytic 
phage replication cycle. The citrate compounds binds calcium and prevents the assembly of progeny phage 
particles and thereby prevents bacterial lysis.  
Once the recipient strains had been infected by the transducing bacteriophages, they were harvested from the 
mixture by centrifugation. The bacteria-containing pellet was resuspended in sodium citrate before they were 
plated onto selective TSA containing additional sodium citrate. The bacteria were then grown at 37oC for 
about 48 hours. The bacteria that were transduced with the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid would, in 
addition to the fluorescent gene also be carrying an antibiotic resistance gene, which allowed them to grow 
on the selective media.  
To verify that the bacteria that grew on the selective media actually contained the fluorescent gene-carrying 
plasmid, the colonies were tested for their fluoresces in the Leica DMLS fluorescent microscope. The 
bacterial plasmid is, as it is seen in figure 10, not integrated into the recipient bacterial chromosome, but is 
floating in the bacterial cytoplasm. 
          4.3.3.2.1. Stability of Plasmid 
To determine whether the fluorescently transduced recipient strains, which were obtained from 
the transduction, could be used in in vitro and in vivo experiments, the stability of the plasmids 
were tested. A growth experiment performed with and without appropriate plasmid-maintaining 
antibiotics was conducted to test the stability of the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid 
(Figure 11). The transduced fluorescent bacteria were streaked onto both TSA and TSA 
==PAGE:28==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
23/66 
 
supplemented with the appropriate antibiotic several times, and the stability of the fluorescence 
in the growing colonies were obtained through the Leica DMLS fluorescent microscope.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4.4. In Vitro Biofilm Formation  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Two of the obtained pJEBAN3-carrying clinical S. aureus strains were used in an in vitro biofilm formation 
experiment. The un-tagged clinical S. aureus strains were used as well (Table 5). 
 
 
Figure 11 An illustrative picture showing how the stability of the fluorescent protein-
expressing plasmid was examined. The transduced bacteria, obtained from the bacteriophage-
mediated transduction, were restreaked onto both TSA and TSA supplemented with 
appropriate antibiotics. The Leica DMLS fluorescent microscope was used to examine the 
stability of the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid-containing colonies.  
Figure 12 A schematic overview of the biofilm-system setup used in this study. The 
upper media bottle contains AB media supplemented with 6 μg/mL erythromycin, 
whereas the lower media bottle contains AB media.

The biofilm formation of the strains were grown and analysed in a flow-chamber biofilm-system, which is 
described by Tolker-Nielsen & Sternberg, (2011). The setup was assembled and sterilized according to the 
protocol found in Tolker-Nielsen & Sternberg, (2011). In this experiment the setup was a little different to 
the standard setup, because the experiment was performed with different strains that needed different media 
(Figure 12). The tagged strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) were grown in AB media supplemented 
with erythromycin (to maintain the pJEBAN3 plasmid) while the non-tagged strains (IMG 167 and IMG 
171) were grown in AB media.  
Both the tagged and non-tagged strains were used in the same setup, to enable comparison between the 
produced biofilms, and thereby possibly evaluating if the biofilm formation produced by the tagged strains 
seemed to be influenced by the obtained fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid.  
The strains also have different genotypes (Table 4), which allows for comparison of the produced biofilm 
between an ica-positive and –negative strain. 
The strains that were inoculated into the flow chambers were prepared in the following way. The strains 
were streaked from frozen stock onto TSA (supplemented with an appropriate antibiotic if needed) and 
incubated O/N at 37oC. A colony was then inoculated from the TSA plate into TSB (supplemented with an 
appropriate antibiotic if needed) and then incubated O/N at 37oC. The liquid culture was then diluted 
according to table 6 and 250 μl of the culture were carefully injected into each flow chamber with a syringe 
(The media flow was cut off before inoculation). The flow chamber was turned upside down (glass surface 
down) for about 10 – 30 min. post inoculation, to promote the initial bacterial attachment to the slide glass, 
before the flow cell was flipped back and the media flow was started. The pump was set to pump media 
through the system with a speed of 1.75 rpm, which is equivalent to a 100 mL-flow pr. 24 hours. A detailed 
protocol that describes the work with the flow-chamber biofilm system is found in appendix N.  
The biofilm were all grown for 4 days before the biofilm formation was observed via the LEICA TCS SP5 
confocal laser scanning microscope (CLSM).

Before the biofilm formation produced by IMG 167 and IMG 171 were observed in the microscope, SYTO9

4.5. In Vivo Rat Prosthesis Model 
The two yfp-fluorescently tagged clinical S. aureus strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) that were used 
in the in vitro experiment were also used in an in vivo rat experiment. The rat prosthesis model was 
originally developed to study the pathology of infection after orthopaedic implant surgery [Søe et al. 2013]. 
In this study, the rat prosthesis model was used to investigate the biofilm formation that is generated when a 
S. aureus infection is present after a knee prosthesis was implanted into a rat.  
     4.5.1. Preparation of the Strains  
The YFP-expressing strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) that were included in the in vivo 
experiment were streaked onto TSA supplemented with erythromycin for plasmid maintenance, and 
grown O/N at 37oC. A colony was inoculated into fresh TSB media supplemented with erythromycin and 
grown O/N at 37oC at 200 rpm. The OD600 of the liquid O/N cultures were measured, and the cultures 
were diluted to obtain a concentration of 103 CFU pr. 100 μl (OD600 = 0.1 is approximately 108 CFU/mL). 
A study performed by Søe et al. (2013) had shown that an inoculum of 103 CFU would produce a 
moderate infection in lab rats, while the inserted knee prosthesis would remain in situ. Once the cultures 
were diluted to the desired concentration, a CFU-count was performed on TSA, to ensure that the exact 
amount of CFU present in the inoculation volume was known. After this, the bacteria were ready to be 
inoculated into the rat.  
4.5.2. The Operation 
10 rats were used in total for this in vivo experiment: 2 control rats and 8 rats of which 4 were inoculated 
with KMJ167-YFP and 4 were inoculated with KMJ171-YFP, as seen in table 7. All rats were daily treated 
with erythromycin intraperitoneally, to maintain the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmids in the strains.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 13 Pictures taken from the rat knee prosthesis replacement surgery (A-E). A = The left rat knee. B = 
Medial incision cut. C = The femur and tibia condyles was removed and a channel was drilled into medullary 
canal.  D = The femur prosthesis was inserted. E = 14 days post surgery, a fatal blood sample was taken. F = An 
X-ray picture that illustrates how the prosthetics were inserted [Søe et al. 2013]. 
 
==PAGE:31==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
26/66 
 
Before the operation began, all rats were sedated with 1.1 ml hypnorm dormicum (anesthetic). About 10-15 
minutes after the anesthetic was given, a rectal swab was made, which was subsequently used to test whether 
the added erythromycin showed to have an effect on the intestinal flora of the rat. Once the rats were fully 
sedated, the knee replacement operation began. All rats had their left knee joint replaced by a prosthetic knee 
joint (Figure 14). The operation was performed under sterile conditions. 
As it is seen on figure 13.B, a 2-3 cm medial incision was made in the centreline of the knee penetrating both 
the skin and the underlying medial muscles, opening up to the knee joints. Figure 14 gives a simplified 
illustration of the steps used when replacing the knee joint with femoral and tibial prosthetics. The condyles 
of both femur and tibia were cut off, to make room for the prosthetics. A manual drill was used to drill a ≈ 
0.75 cm channel through the remaining condyle and into the medullary canal in both femur and tibia. The 
channels were cleared from blood, marrow and hematoma with a syringe, before 50 μl of the diluted bacteria 
was injected into both the femur and tibia channel. The polyethylene part of the prosthesis was inserted into 
the tibial bone, while the metal part of the prosthesis was inserted into the femoral bone. Once the prosthetics 
was correctly inserted, and the mobility of the knee was tested, the surgical wound was stitched back up, and 
the rat was left to wake up.   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
A description of the ten different rats and the drugs that were used during the operation is listed in table 7. 
The 10 rats were assigned into three groups (I, II, and III), which encompass the control rats, the rats that 
were inoculated with the KMJ167-YFP, and the rats that were inoculated with the KMJ171-YFP. 
A detailed description of the rats and the surgical procedure is found in appendix D.  
 
 
Figure 14 A simplified illustration of the steps used during the knee replacement surgery. 1+2 = The 
femoral and tibial condyles are removed, 4 = A channel is drilled into the medullary canal, 5 = The 
S. aureus MN8 strains is injected into the medullary canal, and 6 = The prosthetics are inserted.

operation 
 
The rats from the three different groups were kept separate during the entire experiment to exclude sharing of 
bacteria. The rats were injected with erythromycin every day until 14 days post surgery, were the rat were 
euthanized. No additional pain relief were given after the rats woke from the anaesthesia.  
==PAGE:33==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
28/66 
 
         4.5.2.1. Erythromycin In Rats 
According to Sørensen et al., (1978), erythromycin is able to penetrate bones, hence 
access the medullary canal where the fluorescent S. aureus strains were injected. By 
daily intraperitoneally injecting 15 mg of erythromycin pr. kg rat pr. day, the 
concentration of erythromycin in the medullary canal would not exceed 15 μg/ml. The 
two stains that were used in this experiment, were tested to see if they were able to grow 
in those erythromycin concentrations.  
4.5.3. Post Surgery 
7 and 14 days post surgery, knee X-rays were performed. Before the rat knee was reopened 14 days post 
surgery, an additional rectal swap was performed. The wound was reopened and visually assessed before the 
femur and tibia implants were removed. The prosthetic implants were kept in 50% glycerol and stored at -
80oC until further studies were performed.  
Before the rats were euthanized, the following samples were taken and send to the Department of Clinical 
Microbiology at Rigshospitalet where they were analysed: A rectal swap, a swap from the synovialis, femur 
and tibia, and a blood sample taken directly from the heart. All rats were euthanized when the blood sample 
was drawn directly from the heart.

4.6. Chromosomally Integrating the Fluorescent Protein-Expressing 
Sequence into Clinical S. aureus Isolates 
 
Six different single-copy integration vectors (Table 8, Figure 22) that carry a constitutively expressed 
fluorescent reporter gene were used to integrate fluorescent reporter genes into the chromosomes of the 
S. aureus isolates. 
 
 
Prior to the beginning of this part of the project, single-copy integration vectors was created and 
provided by laboratory technician Susanne Kofoed. A short description of the fluorescent gene-carrying 
single-copy integration vectors, and the process used to construct them is found in the section below. 
4.6.1. Fluorescent Gene-Carrying Single-Copy Integration Vectors  
The fluorescent gene-expressing integration vectors listed in table 8, were constructed by ligating one of 
the integration vector (pCL25, pLL29 or pLL39 (Table 9, Figure 15)) together with one of the 
fluorescent reporter gene-carrying plasmids (pAH9 or pCM11 (Table 9, Figure 16)). Both the 
integration vector and the plasmid were double digested with HindIII and EcoRI prior to the ligation.  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Simplified illustrations of the undigested fluorescent gene-carrying plasmids (pAH9 and pCM11), and 
of the undigested integration vectors (pCL25, pLL29 and pLL39) are found below.

4.6.2. Purification of Vectors 
The fluorescent gene-carrying integration vectors (Table 8) were purified from E. coli MT102, which they 
were provided in, using the NucleoSpin Plasmid Quick Pure Kit for E. coli, Fermentas. 
A single colony from the integration vector-containing E. coli MT102 strains were inoculated into 10 mL LB 
+ 25 μg/mL spectinomycin and cultivated O/N at 37°C at 200 rpm. 3 mL of the O/N culture were spun down 
and the supernatant were discharged. The integration vectors were then purified from the pellet according to 
the manual provided by the manufacturer. During the elution step, 0.1 mM EDTA was used instead of the 
suggested elution buffer. The purified plasmids were stored at -20°C. 
4.6.3. Restriction Analysis 
To ensure that the purified vectors, that were originally constructed by laboratory technician Susanne 
Kofoed, actually contained the ~1,000 bp long gfp or mCherry-carrying sequence from pCM11 and pAH9, 
respectively, two restriction analyses were performed.  
HindIII and EcoRI Restriction Analysis 
The first restriction analysis was performed with the fast digestive (FD) restriction enzymes HindIII and 
EcoRI (Manufactured by Fermentas), and a Y buffer. The composition of the double digestion mixture was 
mixed according to the table 10 below:
