4.6.3.1. Gel Electrophoresis 
A 1% agarose gel (Protocol, Appendix E) was used to separate and subsequently used to analyse the DNA 
fragments sizes that were obtained from the two restriction enzyme digestions. The digested samples were 
mixed with 2 μl 6 % loading dye before the samples were loaded into the wells. Size-appropriate ladders 
were also loaded. 
4.6.4. Transforming S. aureus RN4220  
The purified vector samples were subsequently transformed into different S. aureus RN4220 strains. To be 
able to transform the S. aureus RN4220 strains, it was necessary to make the cells electrocompetent. 
Electroporation was subsequently used to transform the electrocompetent cells.  
==PAGE:38==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
33/66 
 
4.6.4.1. Electrocompetent S. aureus cells 
In the attempt to create electrocompetent S. aureus RN4220 cells, two protocols were used (Appendix F and 
G). The first protocol (Appendix F) yielded electrocompetent cells that provided an extremely low 
transformation success rate, which is why the second protocol (Appendix G) was used instead. The second 
protocol was developed and enhanced by Ph.D. Sara Bayard Dühring during her Ph.D.-studies in 2008. A 
detailed step-by-step description of the process used to obtain the electrocompetent strains is found in 
appendix G. 
S. aureus RN4220 were streaked onto TSA and grown O/N at 37°C. A single colony were inoculated into 10 
mL TSB and cultivated O/N at 37°C and 200 rpm. The O/N culture was diluted in not less than 200 mL pre-
heated (37°C) TSB to reach an OD600 ≈ 0.05. The diluted culture was grown to OD600 = 0.5-0.6 at 37°C and 
140-190 rpm. The cells were harvested by centrifuging at 3900 rpm at 2-4°C, and subsequently the pellet was 
washed several times in ice-cold sucrose, according to the protocol. After the numerous washing steps, the 
pellet was resuspended in 10% ice-cold glycerol immediately before they were dispensed and snap-freezed 
into 50 μl aliquots, in -80 °C cryotubes.  
4.6.4.1.1. Snap-Freezing the Electrocompetent Cells 
1-2 hours before the electrocompetent cells were dispensed into aliquots, a plastic box containing 
a 2.5 cm layer of 96% ethanol were placed in a -80°C freezer. A rack containing cryotubes were 
immersed into the ethanol minimum 10 min. before the cell dispersion. The -80°C cold cryotubes 
would provide a snap-freezing of the electrocompetent cells when they were dispensed into 
aliquots. 
4.6.4.2. Improving the Quantity of the Purified Vectors 
A sufficient vector concentration is important for an efficient transformation, and the purified vector samples 
were concentrated via precipitation: 
     4.6.4.2.1. Precipitation of the Purified Vectors 
The purified vector samples were precipitated with ethanol and NaAc according to the protocol 
found in appendix H. 300 μl of the purified vector samples were precipitated into 50 μl. The 
precipitation was performed on all six purified vector samples. Gel electrophoresis was performed 
to verify the presence and concentration of the vector samples. 5 μl precipitated vector sample 
was mixed with 1 μl 6 % loading dye and loaded onto a 1 % gel. A non-precipitated vector 
sample was also loaded for comparison. Size-appropriate ladders were also loaded. 
Construction of KMJpYL 
The goal was to use electroporation to transform the fluorescent protein-expressing single-copy 
integration vectors (Table 8) into the electrocompetent RN4220 strains, where they would integrate 
into the chromosome. But as previously described, an appropriate integrase is needed during 
integrative recombination. As it is seen in table 2, CYL557 (a RN4220 strain that contains a Φ11 
integrase-carrying plasmid pLL2787) was provided prior to the start of the experiment. This strain 
would enable integrative recombination between a Φ11_attP and a Φ11_attB site. 
Some of the integration vectors contain both a Φ11_attP and a L54a_attP site, and to be able to also 
perform integrative recombination at the L54a_attB site in the bacterial chromosome, a RN4220 
==PAGE:39==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
34/66 
 
strain that contains a L54a integrase-encoding plasmid (pYL112Δ19) was constructed. The strain 
was named KMJpYL.  
The NucleoSpin Plasmid Quick Pure Kit for E. coli, Fermentas was used to purify the pYL112Δ19 
plasmid from the E. coli strain it was provided in. During the elution step, 0.1 mM EDTA was used 
instead of the suggested elution buffer. The purified plasmids were stored at -20°C. 
The purified integrase-encoding plasmid (pYL112Δ19) was subsequently transformed into 
electrocompetent RN4220 strain according to the transformation procedure described in the next 
section, and the KMJpYL strain was obtained.  
4.6.4.3. Electroporation 
The electroporation parameters used to electroporate the electrocompetent RN4220 strains (RN4220, 
CYL557 and KMJpYL) were slightly modified during the experiments to further optimize the transformation 
success rate. The protocol that was first used did not produce any transformants, the protocol is found in 
appendix I. The second protocol (Appendix J) that was used to successfully transform the electrocompetent 
strains is described in this section. The protocol was developed and enhanced by Ph.D. Sara Bayard Dühring 
during her Ph.D.-studies in 2008.   
Electroporation Procedure 
50 μl of the electrocompetent cells were thawed at room temperature and subsequently put on ice. The cells 
were then mixed with 8 μl of the precipitated vector samples and transferred to a cold 0.2 mm BioRad Gene 
Pulser electroporation cuvette that was kept on ice. The mixture was incubated in ice for about 1 min., before 
the electroporation was performed:  
The cells were pulsed at 2.5 kV, 100Ω and 25 μF, with a 2.5 msec. pulsation time. Immediately after the 
electroporation, the cells were resuspended in 0.9 mL TSB. After electroporation, the cells are fragile as 
the electroporation causes the lipid molecules in the membrane to shift positions and to create small pores 
through the bilayer of the membrane, and adding fresh TSB directly after the electroporation seemed to 
improve the growth prospects for the electroporated cell.  
The electroporated cells were incubated between 2 and 4 hours at 37°C and 200 rpm, to allow for the newly 
acquired antibiotic-resistance gene (acquired from the integration vectors) to be expressed, before they were 
plated on selective TSA medium (8 μg/mL tetracycline) and incubated O/N at 37°C.  
Negative controls were included in all transformation experiments. In the negative controls the precipitated 
vector samples was replaced by Milli-Q water, and the negative controls were performed to test whether the 
antibiotic concentration in the selective medium were sufficient enough to kill the strains that did not receive 
an integration vector. 
4.6.4.4. Verification of the Transformants 
To verify that the obtained transformants that grew on the selective media, actually were carrying a 
fluorescent protein-expressing integration vector, the colonies were tested for their fluorescence in the Leica 
DMLS fluorescent microscope.  
To ensure that the obtained fluorescent transformants were in fact transformed S. aureus strains and not 
contaminants, they were streaked and grown on CHROMagarTM Staph aureus. A description of the medium

4.6.5. Transferring the Integration Vector into the Clinical S. aureus Isolates 
The last step in the process of chromosomally tagging clinical S. aureus isolates, was to move the 
chromosomally integrated fluorescent gene-carrying integration vector from the chromosome of the 
transformant strains and into the recipient clinical S. aureus strains. The transfer was facilitated by 
bacteriophage-mediated transduction. Figure 18 shows a simplified illustration of the bacteriophage-
mediated transduction that was used in this experiment.

Figure 18 Bacteriophage-mediated transduction. A simplified figure showing the 8 overall steps used when 
transferring a chromosomally integrated vector (which is integrated into the donor bacteria chromosome) into 
the chromosome of clinical S. aureus strain (Recipient strain).  
1 The bacteriophage attaches and injects its phage genome into the bacteria (Donor strain) 
2 Phage-specific enzymes break down the bacterial chromosomes 
3 Phage enzymes and bacterial materials are used to replicate the phage chromosome 
4 Phage genes are expressed and used to produce phage structural components 
5 Progeny phages assemble and DNA is packaged in the phage heads. Subsequently the phage is lysed 
6 The phage that carries the mistakenly packaged bacterial chromosome sequence can infect and inject the DNA 
into another bacteria (Recipient strain)  
7 Integration of the bacterial donor DNA into the recipient chromosome by homologous recombination 
8 Transduced bacterium

4.6.5.3. Bacteriophage-Mediated Phage Transduction 
A detailed protocol that describes the individual steps that were used during the preparation of the phage 
lysate and when the transduction was performed, is found in appendix B.  
4.6.5.3.1. Preparing the Phage Lysate 
The first step in transferring the chromosomally integrated vectors (pSK105 (GFP) and pSK105 (mCherry)) 
from the donor strains and into the recipient strain, was to prepare the phage lysate. The phage lysate is 
prepared exactly like it was in the previously bacteriophage-mediate transduction-experiment (According to 
the protocol found in appendix B). In this experiment the Φ80a-bacteriophage was used instead of the Φ11 
bacteriophage.   
In the previous bacteriophage-mediated transduction experiment the goal was to transfer a cytoplasmic 
plasmid from the donor strain and into the recipient strain, but in this experiment the goal was to transfer a 
chromosomally integrated vector from the donor strains and into the clinical recipient S. aureus strain 
(Figure 18).  
The amount of PFU in the transducing phage lysates was determined before the actual transduction was 
performed. A protocol that described the method used to determine the amount of PFU in the lysate is found 
in appendix B. 
4.6.5.3.2. Transduction  
The prepared transducing phage lysates were then used to infect the recipient strain. A small fraction of the 
transduced phages carried the exact piece of the genome that contained the fluorescent protein-expressing 
integrated vector, and as the sequence is transferred and injected into the recipient S. aureus strain it will 
integrate into the recipient chromosome by homologous recombination (Step 7 and 8 on figure 18).  
The transduction process was performed exactly like it was in the previous bacteriophage-mediate 
transduction experiment, according to the protocol found in appendix B.

Master Thesis, February 2015

38/66 
 
5. Results & Discussion 
The aim of this project was to develop a step-by-step approach to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical S. 
aureus strains, to facilitate investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro and in vivo. The 
obtained approach can also be used in general to modify S. aureus strains, and might even be usable in other 
staphylococcus strains, such as S. epidermidis.    
Investigation of S. aureus biofilm formations in vivo is challenging because the standard procedure used to 
visualize bacterial biofilms is by staining with SYTO9, which stains eukaryotic cells as well as bacteria. 
Genetically manipulating S. aureus is currently a difficult and time-consuming task, because of the highly 
efficient restriction barriers that impede it. Bacteriophage-mediated transduction has greatly increased the 
possibility of successful genetic modifications of staphylococci, and thereby facilitated the study of clinically 
relevant S. aureus isolates in vivo. Fluorescently tagged bacteria has been used extensively for microbial 
research, and the development an approach that can be used to obtain fluorescently tagged S. aureus strains, 
will enable an essential microbial tool that will aid in the in vivo investigation of staphylococcal biofilms 
[Bose, 2014; Olson & Horswill, 2014]. 
As mentioned before, two separate approaches were used to obtain these fluorescent clinical S. aureus 
strains: 
1. Fluorescently tagging clinical S. aureus strains with fluorescent protein-expressing plasmids  
2. Chromosomally integrating a fluorescent protein-expressing sequence into clinical S. aureus 
strains  
5.1. Tagging Clinically Relevant S. aureus Strains with Fluorescent 
Protein-Expressing Plasmids 
5.1.1. Bacteriophage-Mediated Transduction 
The first approach used to obtaining fluorescently tagged clinical S. aureus isolates, was to transfer a 
fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid from one S. aureus strain and into clinical S. aureus strains via 
bacteriophage-mediated transduction. Generalized transduction is a convenient tool that is used to quickly 
and easily to transfer plasmids or chromosomally encoded markers between S. aureus strains. The 
experiment was performed with an yfp-carrying plasmid, pJEBAN3, and a gfp-carrying plasmid, pCM29. In 
addition to the gfp- and yfp-gene, the plasmids also contained an antibiotic-resistance gene. The pCM29 
plasmid contained a chloramphenicol resistance gene, and the pJEBAN3 plasmid contained an erythromycin 
resistance gene. The resistance genes enabled the use of antibiotic screenings and thereby the de-selection of 
the bacteria that did not receive the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid during the transduction.  
Prior to the transduction, the clinical S. aureus strains were therefore tested for their sensitivity towards 
chloramphenicol and erythromycin.  
 
Table 14: Antibiotic sensitivity test on the six clinical S. aureus strains 
Clinical strain  
Sensitivity towards chloramphenicol 
(cam) and erythromycin (erm) 
 
 
IMG 167 
cam > 6 μg/ml 
 
erm ≥ 6 μg/ml 
==PAGE:44==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
39/66 
 
 
 
All strains were sensitive towards ≥6 μg/ml erythromycin and ≥50 μg/ml chloramphenicol.  
5.1.1.1. Phage Lysate 
During the preparation of the different phage lysates, very obvious and big plaques were observed in the top 
agar, which indicate that the Φ11-bacteriophage was efficient in lysing the bacterial donor cells. The lytic 
life cycle results in the destruction of the infected bacterial because the amount of progeny phages becomes 
so high that the bacterial cell wall bursts. An efficient lytic life cycle thus results in a high phage titer in the 
lysate.  
5.1.2.1. Plaque Forming Units 
Once the transduced phage lysate was prepared, the amount of PFU/mL was measured. This measurement 
was then used to ensure an optimal multiplicity of infection (MOI ≈ 0.1), and thereby an optimal S. aureus 
transduction frequency.   
 
 
 
When the amount of PFU in the transducing phage lysate is known, and the amount of CFU in the recipient 
strain culture is known, a MOI of around 0.1 can be obtained. The specific MOI is obtained to minimize the 
chances of double infections and to increase the chances of a successful transduction. 
As it is seen above, the phage titters in the transducing phage lysates are fairly high, which increase the 
chance of a successful transduction. 
5.1.1.3. Transduction 
The transducing phage lysate was used to infect the recipient S. aureus strains. Once the transducing phages 
had infected and injected the packaged DNA into the bacterial cell, sodium citrate was added to prevent the 
progeny phages from assembling. When the life cycle is stopped, the infected bacterial cell will not lyse and 
will live on with the injected phage-carried DNA. After the transduction, the transduced bacteria were plated 
onto selective media, which only allowed that bacteria that were transduced with one of the plasmids 
(pJEBAN3 or pCM29) to grow.  
There was no growth on any of the control transduction-plates, which illustrates that the amount of 
antibiotics that were added to the TSA plates were sufficient enough to kill the bacteria that did not receive 
the plasmid. The bacteria that had received a fluorescent protein-expressing and antibiotic resistance gene-

As it is seen in table 15, none of the clinical S. aureus isolates transduced with the pCM29-carrying phage 
lysate were able to grow on the selective media, while all the transduced clinical strains showed numerous 
pJEBAN3-carrying CFU. The obtained pJEBAN3-carrying strains were named as seen in table 15.  
I do not know exactly why no pCM29-carrying recipient strains were obtained, since the exact same 
bacteriophage and experimental parameters were used for both transductions, but an explanation could be 
that the recipient strains were not susceptible to the pCM29 plasmid or that the concentration of the antibiotic 
in the selective media was too high. An explanation could obviously also be due to an error in the 
transduction approach. 
Since no pCM29-containing recipient strains were obtained, the study continued with the six pJEBAN3-
containing YFP-expressing clinical S. aureus strains.  
5.1.2. Verification of Fluorescence  
The fluorescence of the six pJEBAN3-containing YFP-expressing clinical strains was verified by 
microscopic observation using the Leica DMLS (4x/0.10 lense) fluorescent microscope. All the fluorescent 
clinical strains showed a uniformly and strong YFP expression thought-out the entire colony (Figure 19).  
 
 
==PAGE:46==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
41/66 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
There is a size difference in the colonies seen on figure 19, because some of the colony-pictures were taken 
after one O/N incubation, while others were taken after two O/N incubations.   
5.1.3. pJEBAN3-Plasmid Maintenance is Needed  
The pJEBAN3 plasmid was, as it was shown in figure 10, not integrated into the chromosome of the 
recipient strains following transduction, but was floating in the cytoplasm of the cell. The stability of the 
plasmid was tested, as most biotechnologically used plasmids need to be kept under antibiotic pressure to 
avoid plasmid loss.  
The stability of the pJEBAN3 plasmid was tested by comparing the fluorescence of the strain-colonies that 
were grown on TSA with and without the addition of 6 μg/mL erythromycin.  
5.1.3.1. Restreaking onto TSA 
When pJEBAN3-carrying YFP-
expressing strains were restreaked onto 
TSA, the colonies showed clear signs of 
the strains losing the YFP-expressing 
pJEBAN3 plasmid right away, as the 
fluorecense disappeared as the colony 
grew (Figure 20). All colonies showed 
speckled or snowflake-like fluorescent 
patterns that illustrate segregationally 
instability. After just three restreaks onto 
TSA without erythromycin, the pJEBAN3 
plasmids, and thereby the YFP-
expression, were completely lost.  
 
 
Figure 19 pJEBAN-carrying clinical S. aureus colonies from the initial 
selective plates (TSA+6 μg/ml erythromycin). These colonies all have a 
very strong and uniform fluorescence.  
Figure 20 YFP-expressing clinical S. aureus colonies after being 
restreaked onto TSA. All strains show clear signs of unstable YFP-
expressing plasmids when grown on media without erythromycin. 
==PAGE:47==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
42/66 
 
5.1.3.2. Restreaking onto TSA+6 μg/ml erythromycin 
Fluorescent pJEBAN3-carrying strains were picked directly from the selective plates used during the 
transduction and restreaked onto TSA+6 μg/mL erythromycin. The first five times the colony was restreaked 
onto TSA+6 μg/mL erythromycin, there was no change in the YFP-fluorescence of the colonies. The sixth 
restreaking, showed that KMJ168-YFP started to lose its fluorescence, while the remaining five strains 
seemed to keep their YFP-expression, hence the pJEBAN3-plasmid (Figure 21).  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
After another five restreaks onto TSA+6 μg/mL erythromycin the five stable strains seemed to keep the 
pJEBAN3-plasmid and its fluorescence, while the KMJ168-YFP strain lost its YFP-expressing plasmid 
completely after nine restreaks in total.  
It is important to study the stability of the pJEBAN3 plasmid, as unstable plasmids and fluctuating 
fluorescence could influence results obtained from biofilm-formation studies performed with the fluorescent 
strains. The pJEBAN3 plasmid showed to be segregationally unstable when grown on TSA and the addition 
of erythromycin was necessary to maintain the plasmid. Several explanations can be used to explain the 
instability of the plasmid, but the overall reason why bacteria loose plasmids is if their growth and/or 
survival is hindered by it. Studies performed on several fluorescent proteins, such as GFP and YFP has 
shown that the fluorescent proteins do not appear to have an obvious harmful effect when used to illuminate 
living cells [Shaner et al., 2005]. In contrast to this, the expression of the pJEBAN3-carrying genes (yfp- and 
resistance gene) does exert an extra metabolic load in the cell, which can be the reason why the cells lost the 
plasmid.  
Figure 21 pJEBAN3-carrying clinical S. aureus colonies after being restreaked onto TSA+6 
μg/ml erythromycin six times. All strains except KMJ168-YFP show clear signs of stable YFP-
expression, while KMJ168-YFP seems to lose the YFP-expressing plasmids.  
==PAGE:48==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
43/66 
 
The pJEBAN3 plasmid carries an erythromycin resistance gene, and by growing the plasmid-carrying strains 
in media supplemented with erythromycin, the plasmid is maintained in all strains except KMJ168-YFP. 
When the pJEBAN3-containing strains are grown in the presence of erythromycin the plasmid will 
contribute with an erythromycin resistance gene, and the plasmid will therefore exert a vital function to the 
cell and thereby maintain the plasmid.  
As it is seen in figure 21, the KMJ168-YFP strain seem to lose the YFP-expressing plasmid despite being 
maintained with erythromycin. This instability could be explained by the possibility that the proteins 
expressed from the yfp- and erythromycin resistance gene-carrying pJEBAN3 plasmid could be toxic for the 
bacteria. From what I know about the IMG 168 clinical isolate, I cannot explain why this exact strain loose 
its plasmid, despite being maintained by erythromycin. 
Two of the obtained pJEBAN3-carrying clinical strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) were used in both 
an in vitro flow-chamber biofilm study and in an in vivo rat model study. In both studies erythromycin was 
supplemented to maintain the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid. 
5.2. In vitro Biofilm Formation 
The in vitro biofilm formation study was performed using the following two pJEBAN3-carrying strains and 
the corresponding non-tagged strains.

The two pJEBAN3-carrying strains were included in an in vitro study, because I wanted to examine if there 
were a visible difference in the biofilm formation produced by the pJEBAN3-carrying strains compared to 
the non-tagged strains. The non-tagged strains were also included in the study as a positive control, since 
both IMG 167 and IMG 171 previously have shown excellent biofilm formation when grown in this exact 
setup. 
The difference between the KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP (and also the IMG 167 and IMG 171) is that 
the ica-locus in the KMJ171-YFP (and IMG 171) was replaced with a tetracycline resistance cassette, 
thereby leaving the strain ica-negative. An observation and analysis of the biofilm formation produced by the 
different strains could therefore also reveal if the presence of the ica-operon, hence the production of PNAG, 
had an impact on the biofilm formation in the strains. 
The CLSM-observations that were performed 4 days after the inoculation, showed that all flow cells 
contained detached micro-colonies floating around the chambers (picture not included), but no attached 
biofilm formations were observed in either of the flow cells that containing the tagged strains (KMJ167-YFP 
and KMJ171-YFP) nor the un-tagged strains (IMG 167 and IMG 171). The experiment was performed three 
==PAGE:49==

44/66 
 
times, revealing the same detached micro-colonies every time. Extending the duration of the biofilm growth 
did not change the outcome.  
Since no attached biofilm formation was observed in the chambers inoculated with the IMG 167 and IMG 
171 strains (positive controls that should be able to form at least a thin layer of biofilm within four days), I 
knew that the results obtained from the experiment were questionable. The process of growing and obtaining 
staphylococcal biofilms by using the flow-chamber setup can be challenging and to be able to conclude 
anything from the in vitro study, additional experiments needs to be done.  
5.3. In vivo Rat Experiment 
In an attempt to investigate the otherwise unclear role that staphylococcal biofilms has in bone infections, 
and to study where staphylococci reside and spread in and on the bone and on implants during infections, 
two of the pJEBAN3-carrying S. aureus strains (Table 17) were used to infect lab rats that had undergone a 
knee arthoplasty procedure.

5.3.1. Resistance Towards Erythromycin 
In an attempt to maintain the pJEBAN3 plasmid in the fluorescent strains that were inoculated into the 
femoral and tibial medullary canals in the rats, the rats were daily treated with erythromycin (5 mg pr. rat pr. 
day). To test whether the two fluorescent strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) were able to survive the 
erythromycin concentration in the rat, their growth were tested in both liquid and solid media supplemented 
with up to 200 μg/mL erythromycin.

5 mg erythromycin was given to each rat pr. day. The average rat weighted 340 g., which means that the 
concentration the erythromycin would reach in the rat was approximately 15 μg/ml. According to a study 
performed by Sørensen et al. (1978), erythromycin is able to penetrate bones, and concentrations measured 
in the cancellous bone showed to range from 1/7 to 1/2 of the corresponding serum values. The results from 
table 18 show that both fluorescent clinical strains were able to grow in much higher erythromycin 
concentrations that the once injected into the rats.  
5.3.2. Inoculum Size 
During the rat knee replacement surgery, 50 μl of a putative 103 CFU/100 μl bacteria-concentration was 
injected into the medullary canal in both femur and tibia (100 μl in total). Simultaneously to the injection 
into the rats an actual CFU-count was made on TSA:

The putative concentrations was 103 CFU/100 μl, which means that the inoculum size for KMJ171-YFP that 
were injected into the rats were a little over three times higher than expected. 
5.3.3. The Surgery 
During the rat knee joint prosthetic replacement, the following three rats did not wake up from the 
anaesthetics: Rat no. C1, C2, and 174 (Table 7). The remaining rats showed to have a fast and relative 
painless recovery. The cause of death in the rats that died post surgery did not have any relation to the 
injected bacteria. Rats are very sensitive to cooling, and during the surgery their body temperature dropped a 
lot, which was probably the cause of death. Future rat-surgeries will be performed on hot plates.  
       5.3.3.1. Post Surgery  
The X-rays that were taken 7 days post surgery (pictures not included), were taken to ensure that the 
prosthetic devices were all still fixed into the medullary canal of the bone.  
Dr. Med. Niels Søe Nielsen evaluated the X-ray pictures (Appendix D), and the medullary canals 
showed signs of commencing osteomyelitis.  
The X-rays that were take 14 days post surgery (picture not included) showed that the prosthetics were 
still fixed in the bones, and that the osteomyelitis had worsened compared to what was seen in the 
previous X-ray (Appendix D). The rats (Rat. no. 169 -172) that were infected with the KMJ167-YFP 
==PAGE:51==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
46/66 
 
strain, which is a tagged ica-positive S. aureus MN8 strain showed a slightly higher occurrence of 
osteomyelitis compared to the rats (Rat. no. 173-176) that were infected with KMJ171-YFP, which is an 
ica-negative S. aureus MN8 strain. The evaluation sheet (Appendix D) shows that the overall score of 
the assessment of the X-ray pictures were 3 and 3.5 for the rats infected with the ica-negative and ica-
positive S. aureus MN8, respectively. Both scores indicate a mild infection, but the observations seen in 
the rats infected with the ica-positive strain (KMJ167-YFP) showed slightly increased osteomyelitis, 
and a slightly worse infection score. These observations might illustrate that the presence of the ica-
operon, hence the expression of PNAG in S. aureus MN8 strains, increase or strengthens the biofilm 
formation in vivo and also the osteomyelitis.  
       5.3.3.2. Microbiological Tests 
The results from the microbiological tests (Appendix M) shows that 14 days post surgery, there were no 
signs of S. aureus in the samples taken from either the synovialis, femur or tibia, which illustrate that 
the bacteria were completely eradicated from the rat.  
The microbiological results obtained from the rectal swaps that were taken pre surgery and 14 days post 
surgery, showed that the daily treatment with erythromycin did not seem to cause a detectable change to 
the intestinal flora of the rats.  
The overall results obtained from the in vivo experiment revealed that the KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP 
strains that were injected into the rat during the knee arthoplasty surgery were eradicated between 7 and 14 
days post surgery/infection. The occurrence of a mild osteomyelitis observed in all rats 7 days post surgery 
proves that the S. aureus strains were not killed immediately after they were injected into the rats. The 
aggravation of the osteomyelitis seen from the X- rays taken at day 7 to day 14 post surgery, demonstrates 
that the bacteria were eradicated sometime in between day 7 and 14 post surgery. At day 14-post surgery, all 
the injected bacteria were completely eradicated from the rat.  
According to Dr. Niels Søe Nielsen, the immune system found in rat is very effective in eradicating 
infections because the time it takes the rat immune system to produce cytokines is very low compared to 
humans. The development and function of the immune system depends on the production and response to 
cytokines, which is why the rats are so extraordinary at clearing infections.  
According to Søe et al. (2013), using an inoculum size of 103 CFU will cause persistent infections and 
osteomyelitis in lab rats, but in this experiment supplementation of erythromycin would have contributed 
with an additional pressure to the bacteria, which might have caused for the complete eradication of the 
bacteria.  
The femoral and tibial knee implants were removed from the rats 14 days post surgery, but since the 
microbiological results showed that all bacteria were eradicated, it was not possible to investigate the biofilm 
formation on the implants.  
No definitive observations, as to where staphylococcal bacteria resided and spread on the implant, were 
made from the in vivo study.  
Besides possibly causing some problems with bacterial eradication, the plasmid-maintenance with 
erythromycin might lead to some erythromycin-caused pathological variations in the rats that could influence 
the S. aureus virulence, and thereby the results obtained from in vivo staphylococcal biofilm formation 
studies. From the observations made in this experiment, the necessity of chromosomal integration of the 
fluorescent reporter genes was realized.  
==PAGE:52==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
47/66 
 
5.4. Chromosomally Integrating the Fluorescent Protein-Expressing 
Sequence into Clinical S. aureus Strains 
In the second approach used to obtain fluorescent clinical S. aureus strains, the goal was to integrate a 
fluorescent reporter gene into the chromosome of the recipient clinical S. aureus strains. By obtaining a way 
to integrate fluorescent genes into the chromosome of clinical S. aureus strains, the problems associated with 
the plasmid-instability and unwanted effects related to the antibiotic plasmid-maintenance can be avoided. 
Besides obtaining an efficient microbiological tool to investigate S. aureus biofilm formation in vivo, the 
method behind the approach can most likely be used to facilitate genetic manipulation in S. aureus strains 
and possibly in other Staphylococcus stains as well.   
Genetically manipulation of clinical S. aureus strains is very difficult, and the multi-step process used to 
integrate a fluorescent gene into the chromosome of S. aureus ended up being quite a difficult task. As 
mentioned, the single-copy integration vectors that were used to integrate the fluorescent reporter gene into 
the chromosome of S. aureus were amplified in E. coli. Due to the extremely efficient restriction barriers 
found in S. aureus, it was not possible to transform the integration vectors directly into the clinical S. aureus 
isolates. To overcome this problem the restriction-deficient S. aureus strain, RN4220, was used as the 
intermediate cloning host. Once the fluorescent gene-carrying integration vector was integrated into the 
chromosome in RN4220, bacteriophage-mediated transduction was used to transfer the vector from the 
intermediate host and into the clinical S. aureus isolates where it would integrate into the chromosome by 
homologous recombination. Because the fluorescent protein-expressing integration vector is transferred from 
the chromosome of one S. aureus strain (RN4220) and into another S. aureus strain, the problems associated 
with the restriction barriers were avoided.  
5.4.1. Purification and Restriction Analyses 
The first step in the multi-step process used to obtain the chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-
expressing strains, was to purify the fluorescent gene-carrying single-copy integration vectors from the E. 
coli MT102 strains, and to verify that the vector were carrying the fluorescent gene-expressing sequence.  
During the elution step in the purification process the suggested elution buffer (Elution buffer AE: 5 mM 
Tris/HCl, pH 8.5) was replaced with 0.1 mM EDTA, pH 8. The reason why the elution buffer was changed 
was to minimize the salt-concentrations in the samples. During electroporation is very important that the salt 
concentrations are as low as possible, as a high salt concentration might cause an electrical discharge known 
as arcing, which usually reduce the viability of the bacteria [Miller et al. 1988]. Changing the elution buffer 
to 0.1 mM EDTA did not seem to affect the purification process. 
After purifying the six different single-copy integration vectors from the E. coli strains, two restriction 
analyses were made to verify that the vectors were all carrying the ~1,000 bp long fluorescent gene-carrying 
sequence.    
The following figures illustrate the six fluorescent gene-carrying single-copy integration vectors:

From looking at the vector maps shown in figure 22, the outcome of the restriction analysis was predicted. 
By double digesting the integration vector samples with HindIII and EcoRI, the ~1,000 bp sequence that 
contains the fluorescent reporter gene was cut out of the integration vector, and the gel should reveal two 
bands of which the smallest should be around 1,000 bp.  
The double digested vector samples that were run on the gels (Figure 23 and 24) clearly show that all 
integration vectors, except pSK100, has a ~1,000 bp band, which demonstrates that the single-copy 
integration vectors (except pSK100) carried the fluorescent gene-expressing sequence.  
According to the HindIII and EcoRI restriction analysis, the pSK100 integration vector did not seem to 
contain the ~1,000 bp sequence that contained the fluorescent gene. 
5.4.1.2. KpnL Restriction Analysis 
The second restriction analysis was performed using the Kpnl restriction enzyme. This analysis was done to 
further verify that the integration vectors contained the approximately 1,000 bp long sequence encoding the 
fluorescent gene. Both digested and undigested integration vector samples were run on a 1% agarose gel. For 
comparison, undigested samples of pCL25, pLL29 and pLL39 were also loaded on the same gel. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
From looking at the vector maps shown in figure 22, the outcome of the restriction analysis can be predicted. 
If the KpnI digested integration vectors contained the ~ 1,000 bp sequence that contains the fluorescent 
reporter gene the gel should reveal two bands of which the smallest should be ~ 1,800 bp. The integration 
vectors that does not contain the ~ 1,000 bp sequence (pCL25, pLL29 and pLL39) should reveal a ~ 1,500 bp 
band.  
Figure 25 shows that the smallest bands (~ 1,800 bp) observed from the digested fluorescent gene-carrying 
integration vectors (except pSK100) are larger than the bands observed from the digested pCL25, pLL29 and

pLL39 (~ 1,500 bp). The smallest band observed for the pSK100 integration vector seemed to be only ~ 
1,500 bp long, which demonstrates that this integration vector does not contain the ~1,00 bp sequence that 
contains the fluorescent gene. These results support the results from the HindIII and EcoRI restriction

These results demonstrates that according to the KpnI restriction analysis, all the integration vectors (except

The KpnI digested integration vectors that contain pLL39 (pSK105, pSK105 and pLL39) show a weird

The results obtained from the two restriction analyses indicates that the all single-copy integration vectors, 
except pSK100, seemed to contain the ~1,000 bp sequence that carries the fluorescent gene.  
Even though pSK100 seemed not to carry the fluorescent gene encoding sequence, all six integration vectors 
were used in the following experiments. 
5.4.2. Transformation  
The next step in the process was to transform the purified vector samples into the electrocompetent RN4220 
strains where the attP sites would enable site-specific integration into the corresponding attB site in the 
bacterial chromosome. All the integration vectors contain an L54a_attP site and some of them contains the 
Φ11_attP site as well (Figure 22), which enables the vectors to integrate at either the L54a or Φ11_attB site. 
As previously mentioned, the integrative recombination will only happen if the corresponding integrase is 
present, e.g. if the L54a integrase is present, the site-specific recombination will happen between the 
L54a_attP site on the integration vector and the L54a_attB site in the bacterial chromosome (Figure 26) [Ye 
& Lee, 1989].  
 
Figure 26 A schematic illustration of the site-specific recombination of pLL29 at the L54a_attB site (Left) or at the 
φ11_attB site (Right) [Luong & Lee, 2007]. 
As it is seen in table 2, prior to the beginning of the project I was provided with CYL557, which is a RN4220 
strain that contained the pLL2787 plasmid. This plasmid encodes a phage Φ11 integrase, and will therefore 
be able to mediate site-specific integration between an Φ11 attP and Φ11 attB site. Since the integration 
==PAGE:57==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
52/66 
 
vectors also contained an L54a_attP site, I constructed a RN4220 strain that contains the pYL112Δ19-
plasmid. This plasmid encodes a phage L54a integrase, and will therefore be able to mediate site-specific 
integration between an L54a attP and L54a attB site. 
The obtained RN4220 strain that contained the pYL112Δ19 plasmid was named KMJpYL. 
5.4.2.1. Electrocompetent Cells 
Since different integrase-proteins are needed for the different attP sites on the integration vectors, the 
following electrocompetent cells were produced: 
 
Electrocompetent RN4220 cells 
 
Electrocompetent CYL557 cells 
 
Electrocompetent KMJpYL cells 
The exact same methods was used to create all of the above mentioned electrocompetent cells. 
The first attempt used to create the electrocompetent cells (Protocol, appendix F), yielded electrocompetent 
cells that provided an extremely low transformation success rate.  
The second protocol (Appendix G) that was used to create electrocompetent cells, was almost identical to the 
first protocol, but contained one important step that resulted in the production of electrocompetent cells that 
provided a much higher transformation success rate. In the firstly used protocol the cells were dispensed into 
appropriate aliquots and frozen at -80oC (Freezer), while the second protocol used snap-freezing of the cells 
when they were dispersed into aliquots. By using this technique, or by using dry ice/methanol or liquid 
nitrogen, the samples are frozen so fast that no large ice crystals can form. The formation of large ice crystals 
can cause cell damage, which is very undesirable as the electrocompetent cells are very vulnerable already.    
5.4.2.2. Precipitation of the Purified DNA 
The intensity of the bands that were obtained from the restriction analyses (Figure 23, 24 and 25) revealed a 
relatively low concentration of vectors in the vector samples. As mentioned in table 10 and 11, a relative 
high amount of the purified vectors were used in the restriction analysis experiment, as it was suspected that 
the quantity of the purified vectors were low.  
The concentrations of the vector samples were increased by ethanol precipitation where a 300 μl vector 
sample was concentrated into 50 μl. To verify that the concentration of the integration vectors in the samples 
was actually increased, the samples were run on a 1% agarose gel (Figure 27). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 27 Precipitated vector samples. Precipitated (P) 
==PAGE:58==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
53/66 
 
The gel was loaded with the six precipitated vector samples (5 μl vector sample, 1 μl loading dye), and the 
last well was loaded with an equivalent amount of non-precipitated vector sample (pSK105) for comparison. 
By comparing the intensity of the bands obtained from the precipitated vector samples with the band 
obtained from the non-precipitated vector sample, it seems like the precipitation in most of the vector 
samples were successful. 
5.4.2.3 Electroporation 
The electroporation parameters that were used in the electroporation of the electrocompetent strains were 
also modified during the experiment to increase the transformation success rate. 
Kraemer and Iandolo, (1990) did a study to optimize the conditions for electroporation of plasmid DNA into 
S. aureus, and discovered that using a high voltage setting (2.5 kV) at 100 Ω and a capacitor setting of 25 μF 
revealed the best transformation frequencies. By increasing the voltage during the electroporation, the 
electric field is also increased (12.5 kV/cm). 
Besides changing the voltage-setting, the second protocol differs from the frist protocol by fresh TSB to the 
electroporation cuvette immediately after electroporation, to improve the growth prospects and cell viability. 
The electroporation causes the lipids in the cell membrane to create small pores, making it weak and fragile. 
By adding the recovery medium (Fresh TSB) directly after the electroporation, the cells have a higher chance 
of recovering. A fast and efficient recovery is needed for the cells to survive, because shortly after the 
electroporation, they were plated onto a selective media. In the time between the electroporation and the 
plating onto the selective media, the cells need to recover and to express the newly acquired resistance genes. 
The increased voltage-setting and the addition of fresh TSB directly after electroporation improved the 
transformation success rate significantly, and the method provided transformant almost every time it was 
performed.  
Overall the process of producing electrocompetent cells and the method used to obtain transformants were 
quite a difficult and time-consuming process, but eventually I combined different protocols and methods that 
resulted in the creation of electrocompetent cells that were successfully transformed almost every time the 
electroporation was performed.

5.4.5. Bacteriophage-Mediated Transduction 
The chromosomally integrated vectors were transferred from the donor strains (obtained transformants) and 
into the recipient strains (clinical S. aureus isolates) via bacteriophage-mediated transduction.  
The bacteriophage used in this experiment was the Φ80a bacteriophage. The reason why I did not use the 
same bacteriophage as in the previous bacteriophage-mediated transduction, was that the phage lysate 
preparations performed with the Φ11-phage was unsuccessfully prepared in the CYL557 transformants. The 
Φ11 phage was not able to replicate via lytic cycles within the transformant strains (donor strains), and no 
plaques were formed during the phage preparation. The reason for this could be that the pLL2787-containing 
transformants (CYL557 transformants), which carries a Φ11-integrase encoding plasmid, would promote 
replication via the lysogenic cycle. The Φ11 phage is a temperate phage that is able to reproduce through 
both the lytic and lysogenic cycle. When the Φ11 phage injects its genome into the donor strains, the 
overexpressed Φ11-integrase (expressed from the pLL2787 plasmid) will then enable integration of the Φ11-
genome into the Φ11_attB site in the chromosome, hence it becomes a lysogen. If this is the case, it makes 
sense that no plaques were obtained during the Φ11 phage lysate preparation.  
Because of the observed problems with the Φ11 phage, the Φ80a phage was used instead. 
5.4.5.1. Phage Lysate 
As previously mentioned, due to time limitations only two out of the 7 obtained transformants were used as a 
donor strain: KMJpYL104 and KMJpYL105.   
The Φ80a phage was able to infect the donor strains and lyse them – revealing plaques in the top agar.  
The plaques that were observed on the plates where Φ80a had infected and lysed the donor strains were very 
small in size. The amount of PFU/mL in the transducing phage lysates was low: 
 
 
 
With such a low amount of PFU/mL, the chance of a successful transduction is decreased.

The obtained transducing Φ80a phage lysates were used to transduce the recipient strain (IMG 167), but

Due to time limitations, I was not able to perform the transduction again or to enhance the parameters that 
would increase the chances of a successful transduction.

6. Conclusion 
The purpose of this project was to develop a step-by-step approach to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical S. 
aureus strains, to facilitate investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro and in vivo. This 
obtained approach could also be used in general to genetically modify S. aureus strains, and might even be 
usable in other Staphylococcus strains, such as S. epidermidis. 
In the first approach used to obtain fluorescently tagged S. aureus strains, a fluorescent protein-expressing 
plasmid (pJEBAN3 and pCM29) was transferred from one S. aureus strain and into the clinical S. aureus 
strains of interest. The obtained fluorescent clinical S. aureus strains, showed to express a consistent and 
uniform fluorescence when maintained by appropriate antibiotics. One of the fluorescent strains (KMJ168-
YFP) seemed to loose its fluorescence despite being maintained by appropriate antibiotics.  
Two of the fluorescent strains (KMJ167-YFP and KMJ171-YFP) that showed stable fluorescence when 
maintained with antibiotics were used both in an in vitro flow-chamber biofilm system and in an in vivo rat 
model experiment. 
No usable results were obtained from the in vitro flow-chamber biofilm system experiment, as no attached 
biofilm formation was observed in the chambers.  
The fluorescent strains that were used in the in vivo rat model experiment were maintained by treating the 
rats with erythromycin throughout the entire 14 days of the experiment. According to Søe et al., (2013), an 
inoculum size of 103 CFU is enough to cause a persistent infection and osteomyelitis in lab rats, but in this 
experiment, the addition of the plasmid-maintaining antibiotic caused for complete eradication of the 
bacterial strains within less than 14 days.  
The results obtained from the X-rays taken 7 days post surgery/infections revealed commencing 
osteomyelitis, and the X-rays taken 14 days post surgery/infection revealed an exacerbation of the 
osteomyelitis. From these observations it can be concluded, that the osteomyelitis-causing S. aureus strains 
were present in the rats for at least 7 days post surgery/infection. The results obtained from the 
microbiological tests clearly showed a complete eradication of the bacteria 14 days post surgery/infection.  
The assessments of the X-rays (Appendix D) showed a slightly higher infection score in the rats that were 
infected with KMJ167-YFP (S. aureus MN8, Ica+) compared with the rats that were infected with KMJ171-
YFP (S. aureus MN8, Ica÷). The degree of osteolysis was also slightly increased in the KMJ167-YFP 
compared with the KMJ171-YFP. These results allude that the expression of the ica-operon, hence the 
production of PNAG, might have a positive effect on the biofilm formation and the osteolysis. The infection 
score and the degree of osteolysis in KMJ167-YFP was not significantly higher that in KMJ171-YFP, and 
further investigation is needed to be able to conclude whether the expression of the ica-operon affects the 
biofilm formation and infection.  
The fluorescent strains were mainly constructed to obtain a microbiological tool that could be used to 
investigate the staphylococcal biofilm formation in vivo, but the results obtained from the in vivo rat model 
experiment demonstrated that the fluorescent strains were completely eradicated 14 days post surgery, which 
is probably due to the erythromycin that the rats were treated with. Even though the fluorescent strains were 
resistance towards erythromycin, a mixture between the highly efficient rat immune system and the 
supplementation of the antibiotic resulted in complete eradication.  
The supplementation of the plasmid-maintaining antibiotic (erythromycin) might also cause for some 
==PAGE:64==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
59/66 
 
pathological variations in the rats that influence the infection and thereby the results obtained from the 
staphylococcal biofilm formation study.  
Overall, the S. aureus strains that was tagged with a fluorescent protein-expressing plasmid were not usable 
in in vivo studies, and from the observations made in this experiment, the necessity of chromosomal 
integration of the fluorescent reporter genes was realized if the strains were to be used to investigate biofilm 
formation in vivo. 
The fluorescently plasmid-carrying tagged clinical S. aureus strains might provide a useful tool in the in vitro 
investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation.  
Due to the problems associated with the possible antibiotic-acquired pathological variations in the rats and 
the unclear impact that the fluorescent protein-expressing plasmids have on the biofilm mode of growth, the 
development of an approach that would facilitate chromosomal integration of a fluorescent reporter gene into 
clinical S. aureus isolates was perceived. 
The approach used to obtain clinical S. aureus strains with a chromosomally integrated fluorescent reporter 
gene, was a multistep process that included the use of the restriction deficient RN4220 strain as an 
intermediate host. The fluorescent gene-carrying single-copy integration vectors that were used in this 
experiment were able to facilitate site-specific integrative recombination into the RN4220 chromosome when 
appropriate integrase-proteins were present. In total 7 different fluorescent RN4220 transformants that 
contained chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-expressing vectors were obtained.  
The chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-expressing vector was then transferred via bacteriophage-
mediated generalized transduction from the intermediate S. aureus-cloning host (RN4220) and into the 
clinical S. aureus strains of interest. The transfer of the integrated vectors into the clinical S. aureus strains 
were not accomplished in the first try, and due to time limitations I was not able to repeat the experiment. 
Even though the fluorescent protein-expressing integration vectors were not transferred into the clinical S. 
aureus strains, the successful integration into the RN4220 intermediate cloning hosts demonstrates that the 
approach can be used to chromosomally tag S. aureus strains.  
The approach can be used to genetically manipulate S. aureus strains, and possibly also used to genetically 
manipulate other Staphylococcus strains.  
7. Future Perspectives 
A short-term future perspective would be to complete the last step in the bacteriophage-mediated generalized 
transduction that would transfer the chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-expressing vector from the 
intermediate strain and into the clinical S. aureus strains of interest. This would definitively demonstrate that 
the chromosomally integrated fluorescent protein-expressing vector from the intermediate cloning host could 
be transferred, integrated and subsequently expressed in the selected clinical S. aureus strains. 
Once fluorescent chromosomally tagged clinical S. aureus strains were obtained, they would provide a great 
microbiological tool for the investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation both in vitro and in vivo. 
The pLL29 and pLL39-integration vectors that were used in this experiment allowed for integrative 
recombination into two different attB-sites in the chromosome; Φ11- and L54a-attB site, while the pCL25- 
integration vector only allowed for integrative recombination into the L54a-attB site. 
==PAGE:65==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
60/66 
 
The L54a-attB site is located within the geh gene in the S. aureus chromosome. The geh gene encodes a 
lipase that might influence the virulence of the bacteria if disrupted. The Φ11-attB site is located in an 
intergenic region, which according to Ye & Lee, (1989) has shown not to influence the bacterial phenotype. 
The pLL39-integration vector is different from the pLL29-integration vector because it contains a T1 
transcriptional terminator repeat (T15) at the EcoRI site of the vector, which is constructed to prevent read 
though into the cloning site from an external promoter. As it is seen in figure 22, the T15 is located after the 
fluorescent-gene-carrying insert and will therefore not prevent read through from external promoters. 
Since the L54a-attB site is located within a gene that might influence the virulence of the host bacteria, I 
would if possible precede the experiments with strains that contained Φ11-attB-integrated fluorescent 
protein-expressing vectors. 
Verify Correct Integration 
To verify that the different integration vectors had integrated at the presumed attB-sites in the S. aureus 
strains, a PCR analysis should be made. This is done by designing PCR primers that flank the integration 
vector, and subsequently to analyze the amplified sequence. 
Analysis of the growth and virulence of the chromosomally tagged strains 
Before indulging in any experimental studies, it would be advisable to examine whether the strains that 
contain the chromosomally integrated vector seemed to have a changed growth and virulence, when 
compared to the non-tagged strains. 
This is especially advisable in the strains where the integration vector was inserted into the L54a-attB site 
(the geh gene).  
Biofilm Formation Investigation in vitro and in vivo 
The main goal in the construction of the chromosomally tagged clinical S. aureus strains was to facilitate 
investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation in vitro and in vivo. The standard procedure used to 
visualize bacterial biofilms is by staining with SYTO9, but as previously described this stain will also dye 
eukaryotic cells, which is why the investigation of staphylococcal biofilm formation in vivo has been so 
challenging. 
Rat Knee Prosthesis Model: The specific role of staphylococcal biofilm formation in bone infections 
(osteomyelitis) is still unclear, and by including the chromosomally tagged S. aureus strains in the rat knee 
prosthesis model, information about where staphylococci reside and spread in and on bones and where they 
grow and spread on implants could be obtained. The experiment would also reveal information about the 
general pathology of S. aureus bone and bone marrow infections. 
Osteomyelitis is a big problem in S. aureus implant related infections, and additional information about the 
staphylococcal infection might aid in the development of a treatment mechanism that will eradicate already 
established infections or possibly a preventive mechanism that will prevent the infections from occurring. 
ica-operon: Since the expression of PNAG has been found to be essential for biofilm formation in several S. 
aureus and S. epidermidis strains, it could be interesting to compare the in vivo biofilm formation between an 
ica-negative strain and the wild type strain to see if there were a significant change in the biofilm formation, 
and just in general to obtain further knowledge about the ica-operon and the expression of PNAG. This is 
==PAGE:66==
Master Thesis, February 2015  
 
Kristine Marie Jakobsen (s083655) 
 
61/66 
 
another experiment where the rat prosthesis model developed by Søe et al. (2013) could be useful.  
Utilize the Approach to Generally Tag Staphylococci 
The developed approach used to obtain fluorescently tagged clinical S. aureus strains might work in general 
to tag Staphylococcal strains. It could be interesting to see if the approach would work with other reporter 
genes than the fluorescent GFP and mCherry. 
The overall goal, once chromosomally tagged S. aureus strains are obtained, is to utilize the developed 
approach to investigate staphylococcal infections and biofilm formation in vitro and in vivo, in the hops of 
obtaining knowledge that will lead to the development of better biofilm treatment strategies.

Appendix A: Regulation of Biofilm Development 
Biofilm Regulation by Genetic Factors (arg and sarA) 
S. aureus biofilm growth is regulated by several complex genetic factors. As mentioned before the 
major global regulators arg and sarA are involved in many different regulatory systems in S. 
aureus. Both global regulators (arg and sar) have been implicated in S. aureus biofilm formation. 
They act together with sigB by 
promoting either the formation or 
dispersal of biofilm (Figure 1). sarA 
and sigB both work by inducing the 
formation of biofilm, while the arg 
promotes the upregulation of virulence 
factors, that subsequently lead to 
dispersal and further spread of the 
pathogen [Archer et al., 2011].  
sarA and sigB regulation 
SarA is a global regulator that works 
as a central regulatory element that 
regulates the synthesis of S. aureus 
virulence determinants, and has an 
important role in the biofilm formation 
process. SarA is a DNA-binding 
protein that positively regulates the 
expression of factors that promote 
biofilm formation and negatively 
regulates exoenzymes (such as 
thermostable nucleases and proteases) 
that inhibit biofilm formation. As seen 
in figure 1, SarA inhibits the 
expression of proteases and 
thermostable nucleases, which result 
in the formation of an immature 
biofilm. If SarA did not inhibit the 
expression of the exoenzymes, the formation of the immature biofilm would be inhibited. Once the 
immature biofilm increase its cell density, by increasing the cell growth, mature biofilm formats. A 
mature biofilm express autoinducing peptides (AIPs), which eventually induce the expression of the 
arg gene [Archer et al., 2011; Tormo et al., 2005; Mootz, 2013].   
The expression of sarA is increased during biofilm cell growth compared with planktonic growth, 
and sarA mutants are not able to form biofilms, which is most likely due to the over-expression of 
secreted proteases and nucleases [Mootz, 2013]. 
Simultaneously to this, the synthesis of the sigB gene will also result in a downregulation of 
thermostable nucleases and proteases, and promoting expression of adherence factors such as the 
MMSCRAMS that aid in the initial biofilm formation. Sigma B is an alternative sigma factor that 
not only regulates the expression of adherence factors, but that is also necessary for survival in 
response to a wide variety of environmental changes incl. temperature, oxidative or alkaline stress 
etc. The increase in the expression of adherence factors increases the formation of the initial biofilm 
[Archer et al., 2011; Mootz, 2013]. 
Figur 1 Biofilm formation and dispersal regulation by arg, sarA, and 
sigB. Expression of sarA inhibits the expression of thermostable 
nucleases and proteases, which normally would inhibits the immature 
biofilm formation. sarA expression therefore results in an increase in 
the biofilm cell density. The increase in the biofilm cell density results 
in a mature biofilm that secrete autoinducing peptides (AIP). Once the 
AIP threshold is reached the arg gene is induced, which result in an 
upregulation of the detergent-like peptides, nucleases and proteases. 
This causes the releasing of bacterial cells from the mature biofilm 
hence cause dispersal. The expression of sigB will downregulate the 
thermostable nuclease and protease production, simultaneously with 
promoting the adherence factors such as MMSCRAMS, which will 
result in an increase in cell density, hence induce the mature biofilm 
formation [Archer et al., 2011].  
==PAGE:73==
arg regulation 
The arg quorum-sensing system in S. aureus regulates the expression of virulence factors, and 
subsequent the dispersal of biofilm cells, in response to the expression of the AIPs. S. aureus utilize 
the quorum-sensing mechanism to respond to the environment and to coordinate a group response. 
It monitors and reacts to the levels of self-produced AIPs to produce changes in gene expression 
that influence biofilm formation. During cell growth AIP is synthesized and secreted through a 
poorly understood mechanism, and once the concentration of AIP reach a certain level, hence there 
is a high cell density, the arg gene is induced. Once the arg gene is induced, it results in an 
increased expression of virulence factors such as detergent-like peptides, nucleases and proteases 
that cause the mature biofilm to disperse cells, which contribute to the spread of biofilms around the 
body. The expression of arg is therefor inversely correlating with the cell density/biomass within 
the biofilm. The exact mechanism behind the biofilm disassembly and biofilm-cell dispersal is not 
fully understood, but it is primarily thought that an increased production of exoenzymes result in a 
deleterious effect on the matrix components [Archer et al., 2011, Boles & Horwill, 2008; Mootz, 
2013].   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:74==
 Appendix B: Phage Transduction Protocol  
Phage Transduction 
Protocol was provided by Jakob Krause Haaber 
Preparation of a phage lysate containing phages that carry the mutation to be moved into 
other strains  
1. Dilute the Φ 11-phage (10-1 to 10-9) in 0.9% NaCl, 10mM CaCl2 (the phage needs calcium ions to 
assemble) 
2. Mix 100 µl of each dilution with 100 µl of donor cells (= the strain containing the mutation that 
should be moved into other strains) + 15 µl 100mM CaCl2 
3. lncubate 5 min at room temperature to allow for adsorption of the phage to the bacteria 
4. Add 3 ml molten TSB-top agar (TSB, 0.5mM CaCl2, 0.5% agar) - not warmer than 45 °C (!) 
5. Mix by tipping up and down and then pour the mix onto one TSA plate per tube. Incubate the plates 
at 37 °C ON. 
6. Next day you pick the plates where phages have lysed almost all the bacteria (appear clear because 
of lysis, this means that a large amounts of phages is has been liberated). Usually it is the plates 
where about 10-3 dilution of phages has been used.   
7. Phages are collected from these plates by scraping off the thin, soft top-agar layer (containing both 
bacteria and phages) into -3 ml 0.9% NaCl, 10mM CaCl2 
8. The bacteria are sedimented by centrifugation (7000 rpm for 10 min). 
 
9. Sterilize the supernatant by filtration, use 0.45 µm filters and save it 
10. Following the centrifugation, the phages are present in the supernatant. 
 
Determining the number of phages per ml lysate (= pfu/ml) 
The concentration of phages or plaque forming units (pfu) is important to know as transduction frequency in 
S. aureus is quite sensitive to the ratio between phages and bacterial cells. To determine the pfu, the 
procedure described above is repeated using the phage lysate to be tested. The next day, the pfu/ml of the 
lysate is estimated by counting the number of plaques obtained for different dilutions of the phage lysate. For 
instance, pick two plates where 10-100 plaques are visible and calculate how many phages were present in 
the undiluted phage extract by in-calculating the dilution factor. After estimating pfu/ml, the plates can be 
used to prepare a new phage lysate. 
 
Transduction 
Now the phage lysate is used to move the original mutation into a new strain. This strain is referred to as the 
recipient strain: 
1. lnoculate a TSA plate (containing any relevant antibiotics) with the recipient strain and grow at 37 
°C overnight 
2. Inoculate a liquid culture using a single colony from the plate and incubate at 37 °C with 180-200 
rpm shaking overnight 
3. Make 2 serial 1:10 dilutions of the overnight culture of the recipient strain in TSB + 0.5mM CaCl2. 
Measure the OD600 of the final dilution of the recipient strain and approximate the number of bacteria 
(OD600 = 0.1 is about 108 cfu/ml). An overnight culture of S. aureus is approximately 5x109 cells/ml. 
4. Label two sterile plastic tubes (12 ml Falcon tubes or equivalent) ‘control’ and ‘transduction’. Add 
0.5 ml of the diluted cells to each tube 
==PAGE:75==
5. To the tube labeled transduction, add up to 0.5 ml of titered phage to achieve multiplicity of 
infection between 0.1 to 1.0 and TSB + 0.5mM CaCl2 to a final volume of 2.0 ml. 
6. To the tube labeled control, add 1.5 mI TSB + 0.5mM CaCl2 
7. Incubate both tubes at 20 m 37 °C with shaking 
8. Add 1 ml ice-cold 0.02M Sodium Citrate to each tube and pellet the cells for 20 m at 4 °C, 5,000 x g 
 
9. Resuspend the pellets in 1 ml 0.02 M Sodium Citrate and plate 200 µl aliquots on TSA containing 
2mM Sodium Citrate and the required antibiotic(s). Grow the bacteria at 37 °C for 24-48 hours 
 
Only strains, which are resistant to the antibiotics in the plate, will grow. This means they have had the 
resistance marker transduced into them.  
It is very important that there is no growth on the control plates. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:76==
 
 
Appendix C: Induction of Staphylococcus Prophages 
Induction of Staphylococcus prophages 
 
1) Dilute an overnight culture of the strain from which the phage is going to be induced 1/25 in 5 ml 
TSB (with antibiotic if necessary). Shake at 180 rpm in 37 °C until OD540 ≈ 0.3. 
2) Add mitomycin C (final concentration 2 µg/ml). 
 
(NB! Mitomycin C is a chemotherapeutic agent used in treating late stages of cancer in the 
gastrointestinal tract and breast. Use with precaution and wear gloves and lab coat). 
 
3) Shake slowly (80 rpm) at 32 °C for 4h then take out the sample and leave the tubes on the lab bench 
in RT over night. 
 
4) Filter through 0.2 µm filter. Store the lysate at 4 °C. 
 
 
Determination of phage titre 
 
1) Dilute an over night culture of the recipient strain (normally RN4220 or 8325-4) in TSB to OD540 = 
0.34 (NB! Here it is not necessary to reinoculate and grow to 0.34, but rather you can dilute directly 
to OD = 0.34). 
 
2) Add 100 µl phage (from serial dilutions made in phage buffer) to 50 µl of the recipient strain in a 12 
ml plastic centrifuge tube. (Remember to always make one buffer control, that is mix 100 µl phage 
buffer with 50 µl bacteria to make sure that there is no back ground placks.) 
 
3) Incubate at room temperature for 10 minutes (not longer since then you might have additional lysis 
of cells which will affect the titre). 
 
4) Add 3 ml Phage top agar at 55 °C to the tube and immediately pour over the surface of a Phage base 
plate containing. 
 
5) Incubate at 37°C over night. 
 
Infection of Staphylococcus with phages 
 
1) Dilute the over night culture(s) of S. aureus to be infected 1/50 in 10 ml TSB in a 50 ml centrifuge tube. 
Shake at 180 rpm in 37 °C until OD650 ≈ 0.15. The CFU/ml is now roughly 1*108. 
 
2) Spin 5 ml of the culture in a 12 ml centrifuge tube (2 min at 6000 rpm). Resuspend in 2.5 ml fresh TSB 
and add 2.5 ml Phage buffer. 
 
3) Infect the cells with phage 80a to a multiplicity of infection (MOI = phages per bacteria) of 3:1 to 1:1. 
 
3) Shake slowly (80 rpm) at 32 °C for 4h then take out the sample and leave the tubes on the lab bench in RT 
over night. 
 
4) Filter through 0.2 µm filter. Store the lysate at 4 °C. 
 
==PAGE:77==
 
Transduction of SaPI 
 
1) Dilute an over night culture recipient strain (that is to receive the SaPI) 1/50 to OD540 = 1.4 (since 
this is not a very big dilution and you’re going to use much in step 2 you will need to make sure you 
have enough over night culture). 
 
2)  Add CaCl2 to the diluted bacteria to a final concentration of 10 mM. 
 
3) Add 100 µl phage (from serial dilutions made in phage buffer) to 1 ml of the    recipient strain in a 
12 ml plastic centrifuge tube. 
 
4) Incubate at 37 °C for 20 minutes without shaking. 
 
5) Add 3 ml TSB top agar at 55 °C to the tube and immediately pour over the surface of a TSB plate 
containing a selective antibiotic and 1.7 mM NaCitrate. 
 
6) Incubate at 37 °C over night. 
 
 
 
Media and buffers 
 
NB! When CaCl2 is in a media or buffer then sterile filter it separately and add it after autoclaving the rest of 
the media or buffer. 
 
Phage buffer: 100 mM NaCl, 50 mM Tris (pH = 7.8), 1 mM MgSO4, 4 mM CaCl2 
 
TSB top agar: TSB + 0.4% agar (this is prepared by Jonny in the basement). 
 
Phage top agar: 
20 g/l Nutrient broth n°2 (oxoid), 3,5 g/l agar, 10 mM CaCl2 
 
Phage base plate: 
20 g/l Nutrient broth, 7 g/l agar, 10 mM CaCl2

Appendix E: 1% Agarose Gel 
1% Agarose Gel and Electrophoresis 
1.  [1g. agarose for each 100 mL agarose solution]. The agarose is mixed with 1 x TBE buffer 
2. Dissolve the agarose-TBE mix in the microwave (p50 for about 5-8 min.)  
3. When the mixture is dissolved, let it cooled down to 50oC 
4. Select and assemble a suitable molding kit and well-comb 
5. Make sure that the molding kit is levelled before an suitable amount of the 1% agarose-TBE mix is 
added 
6. Add 10 µl EtBr pr. 100 mL agarose-TBE mixture and mix well. WEAR NITRIL-GLOVES (BLUE) 
WHEN HANDELING EtBr! 
7. Insert the well-comb, and leave the mixture to harden (30-45 min.) 
8. When the gel has hardened, remove the well-comb and the molding kit 
9. If its not already added, add 1 x TBE buffer to the tank – There should be enough to cover the gel 
10. Transfer the gel to the electrophoresis-tank 
11. Load your samples (app. 5 µl sample/ladder + 1 µl 6 x loading dye) 
12. Start the electrophoresis. For products around 1.000 bp, use 50 milliamps for about 30 min. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:89==
Appendix F: Electrocompetent S. aureus Cells 1 
Electrocompetent S. aureus 
Grow an O/N culture of the appropriate S. aureus.  
1. Dilute the O/N culture to OD600=0.05 in 200 mL prewarmed TSB-medium.  
2. Grow the culture to OD600=0.5-0.6 at 37 °C with shaking.  
3. Harvest cells by centrifugation at 4500 rpm for 8 minutes at 4 °C.  
4. Wash cells in 100 mL cold 0.5 M sucrose and leave on ice for 2 hours.  
5. Harvest cells by centrifugations (the same settings) and wash in 50 mL cold 0.5 M sucrose.  
6. Harvest cells by centrifugations (the same settings) and wash in 25 mL cold 0.5 M sucrose.  
7. Harvest cells by centrifugations (the same settings) and wash in 12.5 mL cold 0.5 M sucrose.  
8. Harvest cells by centrifugations (the same settings) and resuspend in 800 μL ice-cold 10 % glycerol.  
9. Dispense cells into 50 μL aliquots and freeze at -80 °C.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:90==
Appendix G: Electrocompetent S. aureus Cells 2 
The protocol was provided by Ph.D. Sara Bayard Dühring 
 
Frees et al. 2003, Molecular microbiology 48(6), 1565-1578 
 
1. Inoculate a TSA plate and grow O/N at 37oC 
2. OR Inoculate a liquid culture at incubate at 37oC and 180-200 rpm O/N 
3. Inoculate 250 mL pre-heated (37oC) TSA to reach an OD600≈ 0.05 (Use a 1L flask) 
4. Grow until an OD600 of ≈ 0,5-0.6 is reached, at 37oC and 120 rpm 
5. Harvest the cells by centrifugation (3900 rpm) for 13 min. The centrifuge should be pre-cooled and 
kept at 2-4oC at all times 
6. Discard supernatant. Wash the cells by dissolving the pellet in 125 mL ice-cold 0.5M sucrose and 
centrifuge for (3900 rpm) 10 min. 
7. Discard supernatant. Wash the cells by dissolving the pellet in 60 mL ice-cold 0.5M sucrose and 
centrifuge for (3900 rpm) 10 min. 
8. Discard supernatant. Resuspend the pellet in 30 mL ice-cold 0.5M sucrose, and incubate 2-4 h on ice 
9. Discard supernatant. Wash the cells by centrifugation for (3900 rpm) 10 min.  
10. Discard supernatant. Resuspend pellet in 15 mL ice-cold 0.5M sucrose and centrifuge for (3900 rpm) 
10 min. 
11. Discard supernatant. Resuspend the pellet in 1mL 10% ice-cold glycerol 
12. Dispense cells into 50 μL aliquots, into cryotubes which have been submerged into -80oC 96% 
ethanol (Snap-freeze the culture) 
13. The competent cells are stored at -80oC 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
 
Appendix H: Precipitation with Ethanol 
Precipitation of DNA  
1. The start DNA volume needs to be 50 µl or above 
2. Add 1/10 vol. 3M NaAc, pH 4.8 (Na+ helps with the precipitation of the DNA) 
3. Add 2½ vol. 96% EtOX and mix 
4. Incubate on ice for 5-7 min. 
5. Centrifuge for 30 min. at 20.000 rpm (or max speed) 
6. Discard supernatant with pipette. The pellet/DNA might not be visible. Spin again, 20 sec., remove 
the remaining supernatant 
7. Wash the pellet with 2½ vol. ice-cold 70% EtOH (Kept in the freezer) 
8. Centrifuge for 5 min. at 20.000 rpm (or max speed) 
9. Discard supernatant. The pellet/DNA might not be visible. Spin again, 20 sec., remove the remaining 
supernatant 
10. The DNA-pellet is dried in speed vacuum, OR by letting the EtOH evaporate at 37oC. Do not let the 
DNA dry completely because it will make it very difficult to dissolve again  
11. The DNA-pellet is dissolved in either water or a buffer (e.g. 1mM EDTA, pH 8). EDTA is usually 
used if the DNA will be electroporated into a bacterium  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:92==
Appendix I: Electroporation Protocol 1 
Electroporation of S. aureus 
1. Thaw cells on ice.  
2. Mix 50 μL competent cells with 3-8 μL DNA and incubate for 30 minutes on ice.  
3. Transfer the mix to a cold electroporation cuvette.  
4. Set the electroporation apparatus to 25 μF, 1.5 kV and 100 Ω.  
5. Place the electroporation cuvette in the electroporation apparatus and give it an electric pulse.  
6. Add 0.9 mL of TSB medium and transfer the cells to 15 mL tubes.  
7. Incubate for 3-4 hours at 37 °C with shaking (around 200 rpm).  
8. Plate appropriate dilutions of cells on selective medium and incubate at 37 °C.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:93==
Appendix J: Electroporation Protocol 2 
Electroporation of S. aureus 
The protocol was provided by Ph.D. Sara Bayard Dühring 
Kraemer and Iandolo 1990, Curr Microbiology 21, 373-376 
 
1. Thaw cells at room temperature and keep on ice (without letting them freeze again) 
2. 50 μL cells are mixed with 0.001-1 μg DNA (I used 8 μL of the precipitated DNA). The mixture is 
transferred to a cold 0.2mm electroporation cuvette (kept on ice) and is incubated on ice for 1 min. 
3. Electroporation: 25μF, 2.5KV, and 100 Ω (Pulsation time ≈ 2.5 msec.) If the BIO-RAD is used, use 
Ec2 and StA 
4. Add 0.9 mL TSB to the electroporation cuvette directly after the electroporation 
5. For plasmids containing tetracycline-, penicillin -, chloramphenicol-resistance genes: Put on ice for 
15 min. before plating onto selective TSA plates 
6. For plasmids containing erythromycin- and kanamycin-resistance genes: Transferred culture to a 15 
mL tube, incubate at 37oC and 200 rpm for min. 1 h, before plated onto selective TSA plates 
The newly transformed cells need some time to express the newly acquired resistance genes, before they 
are plated onto selective media

==PAGE:111==
 
3
-.&/,,%0#$1&'(&($'2&)3"**%$,&
Biofilms are cultivated in flow channels. 
The flow channels are cut/drilled in plexiglass, covered with an object glass to allow inspection 
with a microscope. A flow cell is assembled by adding a thin layer of silicone between the channels 
(sausage-like), without touching the plexiglass with the syringe. Put a glass cover slip on top of the 
silicone and push carefully until the silicone covers the whole area of plexiglass between the 
channels (Figure 1). Allow the silicone to dry before use (preferably over night). 
Silicone tubings with dimensions 1 mm inner diameter, 3 mm outer diameter are connected to each 
end of the flow channel.  
 
40 mm
4 mm
Coverglass
Flow
 
       45678%&-9 3-channel flow cell. 
 
 
:.&/,,%0#$1&'(&($'2&,1,+%0&
Except for the tubes going through the pump, the flow system is assembled using silicone tubing. 
For the pump we use the much stronger Marprene tubing. Media bottle, bubble trap and flow-
channels are connected with tubings as shown in Figure 2. The bubble traps are 5 mL syringes 
mounted on the bubble trap base with inlet and outlet (the inlet holes are situated higher than the 
outlet holes) inside the bubble trap. The syringes are closed in the top with a stopper. 
The effluent media is collected in bottles containing 2,5% CETYL. The effluent-bottles must be 
placed at, or above the level of the flow channels. In this way a leak in a flow channel will result in 
media running out of the leak and not air into the leak, which therefore is more sterile, and do not 
create bubbles that can destroy the biofilm. 
 
 
 
Hints: 
• It’s possible to seal leaks in the system by adding silicone and at the same time 
drying with paper tissue. 
• Place the bubble traps in small trays during the preparation of the system.

Start to fill the flow system, by taking off the stoppers on top of the bubble traps (keep the stopper 
sterile by putting them in 96% EtOH). 
When the bubble traps are filled, put on the stoppers again to fill the rest of the flow system (flow 
channels and effluent tubing). Make sure that all air-bubbles are out of the system by carefully 
tapping the flow cells while the pump is running (5-10 min.). 
To empty the system, just disconnect the bottle with either water or media or hypochloride-solution 
and let the system run at high speed (90 rpm). !
8,&%)/)9(,)0*#0.#,-&#./01#232,&4#
Sterilization of the flow system is done by pumping 0.5% hypochlorite (33 mL 15 Vol % 
Sodiumhypochlorite in 1 L of sterile H2O) into the system and allow to sterilize for 3-4 h.  
:(2-)*+#,-&#./01#232,&4#
The flow system is washed to remove the hypochlorite. This is done by filling and emptying the 
system 2-3 times, as described in “Procedure for filling the flow system”, with 1-2 L of autoclaved 
H2O depending on the amount of flow channels. 
;7**)*+#4&6)(#,-%07+-#,-&#./01#5-(**&/2#
The flow system is filled with media as described and the flow rate is setted to approximately 0.2 
mm/s. This is obtained by setting up the 12-channel Watson Marlow pump at 2.00 rpm and the 16-
channel Watson Marlow pump at 1.75 rpm. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
<"#=*057/(,)0*#0.#./01#5-(**&/2#
Before each inoculation, the flow is stopped and the tubing between the flow channel and the 
bubble trap is clamped off. 
The tubing before the inlet is sterilized with 96% ethanol. A 0.5 mL syringe with needle is filled 
with 250 µL of an overnight culture of the cells (diluted 10-1000 times or to a specific OD 
Hint: 
• If the carbon source is very hydrophobic and/or you are going to run your 
experiment at 30°C or 37°C it is advisable to fill the system with the media and let 
it run overnight at the certain temperature to saturate the silicone tubing before 
inoculation. 
 
==PAGE:114==
 
6
depending on the experiment. Dilute in ABT or 0.9% NaCl). The syringe needle is inserted in the 
tubing as near as possible to the flow channel inlet and the cells are carefully injected into the 
channel. After inoculation the tubing is sterilized with 96% ethanol, and the injection hole is closed 
by adding a thin layer of silicone. To allow the cells to establish on the glass surface turn the flow 
cells with the glass surface upside down. After 1 hour turn the flow cell back, take of the clamps 
and start the media flow.

• Don’t change or install anything on the computer 
• Don’t update the computer  
• Don’t change the temperature in the CLSM room 
• Do never turn off the Microscope separately 
• If you are going to use the heating table, ask somebody that knows about it 
• Turn off the lamps if not in use 
• When turning off the CLSM, turn off the lasers and then close the program  
• Wait 15 min before shutting down the computer and the remote control 
• Don’t turn off the microscope at any time 
• Don’t touch the microscope or the computer if you have oil on your fingers 
• Clean objective and xy-table with ethanol before leaving the CLSM  
• Don’t leave things in the CLSM room

can adjust these manually for each channel under the topic "Channels" in the upper part of the 
window Scan Control.

the settings that were used for that particular image.
